Critique of Mu'tazila and Peripatetic Views on Divine Knowledge
Author critiques Mu'tazilite and Peripatetic views on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Mu'tazilite doctrine of fixed non-existent forms and the Peripatetic doctrine of unification, identifying their logical problems.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila conflate existence with fixedness while the Peripatetics wrongly unify the Necessary and contingent.

The author continues his survey by addressing two more influential schools: the Mu'tazila, who held that God's knowledge consists of non-existent but fixed entities, and the Peripatetics (Porphyry and followers), who advocated unification (ittihad) between knower and known. The author points out damaging logical consequences for each.

Guided Path
1
State the Mu'tazilite position
They said God's knowledge is 'known forms that are non-existent yet fixed in the external' (صور معلومة معدومة وثابتة الخارج).
2
Identify the critique of Mu'tazila
The author says it follows that fixity (ثبوت) is existence (وجود), which is problematic. He claims to have detailed a rebuttal elsewhere.
The Arabic: ويلزم عليهم أن الثبوت هو الوجود
3
State the Peripatetic position
Porphyry, his followers, and the Peripatetics held that God's knowledge is unification (اتحاد) of known things with His essence.
4
Identify the critique of Peripatetics
The author says this implies unification of the Necessary and possible, the immaterial and material, which is false by decisive proofs. He admits the view might have an aspect he does not comprehend.
The Arabic: يلزم عليهم ... اتحاد الواجب والممكن، والمجرد والمادي
5
Compare the two critiques
Both views entail metaphysical confusions: the Mu'tazila confuse nonexistent fixedness with existence; the Peripatetics conflate different ontological categories.

الإدراك؛ على ما يشهد به الضرورة، وأيضا حضور المباين عند مباين آخر لا يعقل إلا بالمقابلة والقيام، وكلاهما منتفيان ههنا. وذهب النصير الطوسي إلى أن علمه المعلول الأول، مع ما فيه الحاضر عنده حضور المعلول وهم يلتزمون ذلك، مستندا بما يقول الفلاسفة من أن الاستعداد كيفية في المادة، ذات إضافة متعلقة بالمعدوم ما دام معدوما واستكماله ،بالغير، ويلتزمونه ويقولون الاستكمال بالغير الذي هو صفة كمالية غير نقص، وانكشاف المباين بالمباين، وهم يلتزمون ويقولون: لم يدل دليل تام على اتحاد العلم أو المعلوم، بل فيه مفاسد لا تخفى، ولا يمكن دفعها، كما سيظهر إن شاء الله تعالى. وذهب المتأخرون إلى أن ذاته تعالى هو العلم، Defense of the Matridi Doctrine and Critique of Later Scholars
Author defends the Matridi view and critiques later scholars' identification of essence with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Matridi doctrine of divine knowledge as a simple attribute with relations and evaluate the objections against it, as well as critique the later scholars' identification of essence with knowledge.

Core Idea
The Matridi view avoids the pitfalls of other schools by affirming a simple attribute with relations, while the later scholars' view collapses God's essence into a mere principle of disclosure.

The author now presents the position of his own school, the Matridiyya, and defends it against philosophical objections. He then turns to the later scholars who identified God's essence with knowledge, showing that this leads to the same problems of distinct disclosure and inability to distinguish opposites.

Guided Path
1
State the Matridi position
Knowledge is a simple attribute (صفة بسيطة) subsisting by God's essence, possessing relation (ذات إضافة), and things are distinguished by the difference of its relations.
Arabic: صفة بسيطة قائمة بذاته تعالى، ذات إضافة
2
Identify the philosophers' objection
They object that relation can attach to non-existent things (since God knows non-existent objects). The Matridiyya accept this, citing the concept of 'preparedness' (استعداد) in matter as analogous.
The Arabic: استعداد كيفية في المادة، ذات إضافة متعلقة بالمعدوم
3
Analyze the Matridi defense
The Matridiyya argue that a complete proof does not indicate unification (اتحاد) of knowledge or known, and that unification leads to indefensible corruptions.
4
State the later scholars' position
They held that God's essence itself is knowledge, like a cognitive form (الصورة العلمية) that discloses all things.
Arabic: ذاته تعالى هو العلم
5
Critique of the later scholars
This implies the disclosure of distinct by distinct, that one thing is the principle for opposites, and that God relates equally to everything, losing the basis of distinction (امتياز).
The Arabic: كيف يكون مبدأ الامتياز؟

Two Views on the Nature of Making (Ja'l)
Author contrasts Illuminationist and Peripatetic views on ja'l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Illuminationist and Peripatetic views on the effect of making (ja'l), and identify which view is being asserted in a given statement.

Core Idea
The Illuminationists hold that making directly creates essence; Peripatetics hold that it attaches existence to essence.

The nature of creation (ja'l) is a foundational metaphysical question. In this passage, the author presents the two main positions: the Illuminationists who believe making affects essence itself, and the Peripatetics who believe it affects the description of essence with existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how definitions and causal explanations work in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
Read the passage and note the names of the two schools mentioned: the Illuminationists (Ishraqiyya) and the Peripatetics (Mashsha'un).
2
Extract the Illuminationist position
According to the passage, the Illuminationists hold that the effect of making is the essence itself. They believe the essence pre-exists in non-being, and the maker brings it out to being.
3
Extract the Peripatetic position
The Peripatetics hold that the effect of making is the description of essence with existence. They interpret 'the essence is existent' as what is created by making.
4
Compare the implications
Consider how each view affects the relationship between essence and existence. For Illuminationists, essence is directly created; for Peripatetics, existence is added to a pre-existing essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من المقامين في كيفية الجعل، أهو بسيط أم مركب ؟ اختلف الحكماء والعلماء في أن أثر الجعل هو الماهية نفسها، أو اتصافها بالوجود؟ فذهب الإشراقية إلى الأول، فعندهم كانت المهيئة بنفسها في بقعة الليس، فأخرجها الجاعل إلى الأيس. والمشاؤون إلى الثاني، والمراد بالاتصاف بالوجود مفاد قولنا الماهية موجودة .
The second of the two stations concerning how the making is: is it simple or composite? The philosophers and scholars differed on whether the effect of making is the essence itself, or its description with existence. The Illuminationists went to the first: according to them, the essence was by itself in the locale of non-being, so the maker brought it out to being. The Peripatetics went to the second, and what is meant by description with existence is the import of our saying 'the essence is existent'.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines two opposing positions in a metaphysical debate about the nature of creation (ja'l). The author states that the disagreement centers on whether the act of making directly affects the essence (mahiyya) of a thing or rather its being characterized by existence. The Illuminationists (Ishraqiyya) hold that the essence pre-exists in a state of non-existence and the maker transfers it to existence—essence itself is the direct object of creation. The Peripatetics (Mashsha'un) argue that making attaches existence to the essence, so that the phrase 'the essence is existent' captures what is created. This distinction is crucial for understanding causal explanation in Islamic thought, as it determines whether essences are eternal or created. The argument uses terms like 'essence', 'existence', and 'making' which are foundational for logical definitions (hadd) in Ilm al-Mantiq, since definitions capture the essence of a thing.

Definition of Muqaddimah in Logic
Author defines 'muqaddimah' and clarifies its usage in logic, citing al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the technical definition of 'muqaddimah' in Islamic logic and distinguish it from its common usage as a simple preface.

Core Idea
Muqaddimah is not a preface—it is the foundational knowledge that opens a science.

In Islamic logic, the term 'muqaddimah' carries a precise technical meaning that goes beyond 'introduction'. The author clarifies this meaning by citing al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq and contrasting it with later innovations. Understanding this definition is crucial because every Islamic science builds upon a set of foundational perceptions that constitute its muqaddimah.

Guided Path
1
Locate the grammatical note
Notice the author begins with the correct pronunciation of 'muqaddimah' (kasra under dal) and cites al-Zamakhshari to settle the dispute. This shows that even terminology requires precision.
Pronunciation matters for distinguishing technical terms from common words.
2
Extract the core definition from al-Sayyid
The author quotes al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq: 'muqaddimah' is 'that which leads to the commencement of the science'. Identify this as the real definition—it refers to the primary perceptions (idrakat) that form the starting point.
These perceptions include basic concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat).
3
Understand the extension to indicators
The author explains that when teaching these perceptions, one must use their indicators (mubayyinat): the effects (ma'lulat) and linguistic expressions (alfaz). Thus, 'muqaddimah' is also applied to these indicators by extension.
This extension is pragmatic, not a change in the core meaning.
4
Contrast with al-Taftazani's innovation
The author states there is no need for a new technical term, as al-Taftazani attempted. This highlights that the original definition suffices, and over-complication is discouraged.
Al-Taftazani's attempt likely added unnecessary distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(مقدمة ) فيها بكسر الدال، والفتح خط صرح به الزمخشري، وقد طولوا في بيان الحاجة إلى المنطق تفسيرها، والحق عند هذا العبد ما قال السيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف: أنها عبارة عما يترتب عليه الشروع في العلم ، وهو حقيقة الإدراكات ، لكن يضطر في تعليمها إلى مبيناتها التي هي المعلولات والألفاظ، فيطلق المقدمة عليها أيضا، ولا حاجة إلى تجديد اصطلاح جديد، كما جدد العلامة التفتازاني
(Introduction) In it, with kasra of the dal, and the fatha is an error as stated by al-Zamakhshari. They have elaborated in explaining the need for logic and its interpretation. The truth according to this servant is what al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may Allah sanctify his noble secret) said: that it is an expression for what leads to the commencement of the science, and it is essentially the perceptions, but in teaching it one is forced to use their indicators, which are the effects and terms, so the term 'muqaddimah' is also applied to them. There is no need for a new terminology, as al-'Allamah al-Taftazani innovated.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'muqaddimah' (introduction) as used in logic books. The author begins with a grammatical note: the word is pronounced with a kasra under the dal (muqaddimah), not fatha (muqaddamah), as al-Zamakhshari declared the fatha an error. Then he mentions that scholars have discussed at length the need for logic and its definition. However, the author prefers the view of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (al-Jurjani) that 'muqaddimah' refers to that which leads to the commencement of a science; it is essentially the primary perceptions (i.e., the basic concepts and assents). But in teaching, one must refer to their indicators, which are the effects and the linguistic expressions, so the term 'muqaddimah' is extended to those as well. The author asserts that there is no need to coin a new technical term, as al-Taftazani attempted. This highlights the difference between the logical meaning of 'muqaddimah' as a preparatory step and its common usage as a preface.

المقدمة
الإدراكات

Key Takeaway

Two Interpretations of Tasawwur in Logic
Commentator expands on the definition of tasawwur and debates its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two competing interpretations of tasawwur and explain the commentator's five reconciliation strategies.

Core Idea
Tasawwur's meaning wavers between absolute and acquired knowledge; the commentator resolves the tension without rejecting the matn.

This passage from a logic commentary examines the definition of tasawwur (conception) given in the matn. The commentator first notes that the words suggest tasawwur equals all knowledge, including presential knowledge of God. But then he argues that the standard view in logic restricts it to acquired knowledge. He offers several ways to harmonize the matn's phrasing with that standard view, revealing how terminological debates are handled in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's definition
Find the phrase: 'Knowledge is conception, and it is what is present before the perceiver.' Note that this seems to equate tasawwur with absolute knowledge.
The definition is given as a rasm (description), not a hadd (essential definition).
2
Locate the commentator's objection
The commentator says the apparent meaning includes presential knowledge (al-huduri), but the more correct view is acquired knowledge (al-husuli). Why is this a problem? Because the standard division of knowledge in logic (tasawwur and tasdiq) assumes acquired knowledge.
3
Enumerate the reconciliation strategies
List the five options: (1) restrict meaning by the context of division, (2) include the Ancient (God) as having both tasawwur and tasdiq, (3) the pronoun refers to absolute knowledge implicitly, (4) reinterpret 'presence', (5) appeal to unseen authoritative sources.
Each strategy attempts to preserve the matn's wording while aligning with technical usage.
4
Evaluate the need for such strategies
Why does the commentator go through this effort? The scope of tasawwur affects what counts as a concept in logical analysis. If it includes presential knowledge, then intuitive knowledge of God would be part of logic, which is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَيَانُ الْحَاجَةِ إِلَى عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ بِوَجْهِ يَنْسَاقُ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهِ بِالرَّسْمِ ) الْعِلْمُ التَّصَوُّرُ ، وَهُوَ الْحَاضِرُ عِنْدَ الْمُدْرِكِ(، أي المجرد عن المادة ؛ إذ لا قوة للإدراك إلا فيه اعلم أن ظاهر هذه العبارة يدل على أن التصور مرادف لمطلق العلم الشامل للحضوري ، والأظهر مرادفته للحصولي، كما هو مصرح في كلام البعض، فإما أن يتكلف ويقال: المراد به العلم الذي هو مَوْرِد القسمة بقرينة التقسيم ، ويقال : إنه يرى القديم تصورا وتصديقا، فإما أن يرجع الضمير إلى المطلق المذكور ضمنا، أو يتصرف في الحضور، وإما أن يقال: لعل المصنف اطلع على كونه مرادفا لمطلق العلم من كلام ثقات هذا الفن، وإن لم نطلع عليه .
Explanation of the need for the science of logic in a way that leads to its definition by description: Knowledge is conception, and it is what is present before the perceiver, i.e., abstracted from matter; for there is no power of perception except in it. Know that the apparent meaning of this expression indicates that conception is synonymous with absolute knowledge that includes presential knowledge, but the more apparent is its synonymy with acquired knowledge, as is explicitly stated in the speech of some. So either one may strain and say: what is meant by it is the knowledge that is the object of division, by the indication of the division, and it is said that He (the Ancient) is seen as conception and assent. So either the pronoun refers to the absolute mentioned implicitly, or one manipulates the meaning of presence, or it may be said: perhaps the author came to know that it is synonymous with absolute knowledge from the speech of reliable authorities of this art, even if we have not come across it.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on the definition of tasawwur (conception) as given in the matn. The author begins by stating that the purpose is to explain the need for logic in a way that leads to its definition by description (rasm). He then quotes the matn: 'Knowledge is conception, and it is what is present before the perceiver, i.e., abstracted from matter; for there is no power of perception except in it.' The commentator then analyzes this definition. He notes that the apparent meaning of this phrase suggests that tasawwur is synonymous with absolute knowledge (al-`ilm al-mutlaq), which includes presential knowledge (al-`ilm al-huduri). However, he states that the more correct view (al-azhar) is that tasawwur is synonymous with acquired knowledge (al-`ilm al-husuli), as is explicitly stated by some scholars. He then presents several ways to reconcile this apparent conflict: (1) It could be said that the intended meaning is the knowledge that is the subject of the division into tasawwur and tasdiq, by virtue of the context of division. (2) It could be said that the Ancient (God) is considered to have tasawwur and tasdiq, so the division includes both types. (3) The pronoun in 'its presence' could refer to the absolute knowledge implied in the definition, or (4) one could reinterpret the meaning of 'presence'. (5) Possibly the author had access to reliable sources that state that tasawwur is synonymous with absolute knowledge, even if the commentator has not seen them. This shows the subtlety of terminological debates in Islamic logic, where the same technical term can shift in meaning depending on context and authorial intent.

Key Terms
التصور (al-tasawwur): The author defines it as 'knowledge that is present before the perceiver', but then discusses whether it means absolute knowledge (including presential knowledge) or only acquired conceptual knowledge. [Note: In standard Mantiq, tasawwur is usually opposed to tasdiq (assent) and refers to simple conceptual knowledge; here the author elaborates on its relation to presential vs. acquired knowledge.]
الحضوري (al-huduri): Presential knowledge, which is direct awareness without representational mediation; included in the absolute meaning of tasawwur according to some. [Note: This term is more common in philosophy and Sufism; in logic it is used to contrast with acquired (husuli) knowledge.]
الحصولي (al-husuli): Acquired knowledge, which is attained through mental representations (concepts); the author says the more apparent view is that tasawwur is synonymous with this type. [Note: Standard in logic; it is the usual counterpart to huduri in epistemology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is synonymous with absolute knowledge including presential knowledge.
B) It is synonymous with acquired knowledge (al-`ilm al-husuli).
C) It is synonymous with both acquired and presential knowledge.
D) It is synonymous with the knowledge that is the subject of division.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that even a foundational term like tasawwur requires careful calibration between its literal meaning and technical usage.

Maturidi Definition of Knowledge and Refutation of Union Theories
Definition and refutation of knowledge theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Maturidi definition of knowledge and analyze the refutation of the union theory using conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a simple state with an intrinsic relation; union theories collapse under their own logical consequences.

This passage tackles the elusive reality of knowledge. The author presents the Maturidi definition and then systematically refutes alternative views, especially the theory that knowledge is union with the active intellect or known object. The refutation employs a rigorous case analysis that shows each possible outcome of union leads to absurdity or fails to be a true union.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Maturidi definition
Find the phrase 'hala basita' (simple state) and note its two components: a state subsisting in the perceiver and an intrinsic relation (idafa). This is the genus and differentia.
The definition is: knowledge is a simple state with a relation upon which discrimination depends.
2
Identify the refutation of conjunction (muqarana)
The view that knowledge is conjunction with the active intellect is refuted by a dilemma: either complete conjunction (nothing unknown) or partial conjunction (divides the active intellect). Both are impossible.
This is a disjunctive argument: either completely or partially; both absurd.
3
Analyze the refutation of union (ittihad) - case analysis
The author divides the possibilities after union into three: unchanged, lost, or completed. Each is examined: unchanged means no difference; lost means either attribute (change) or essence (annihilation); completed means either essence (making knower a genus) or attribute (a coming into being). None constitutes union.
This exhaustive case analysis is a form of conditional reasoning: if union, then one of these; each leads to contradiction or non-union.
4
Assess the overall argument
The refutation is presented as more comprehensive than Avicenna's in the 'Isharat'. The author concludes that union is false.
The validity of the argument depends on the exhaustiveness of the cases, which is claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنازعة حينئذ مع الجمعين الأخيرين لفظية. وإن أراد بديهة كنه العلم الحقيقي الذي هو مصداق هذا المفهوم فالدعوى نظرية، والدليل غير تام؛ لأنا لا نسلم أن المضاف إليه علم النور معلوم بالكنه بالبديهة، إنما المعلوم المعنى المصدري. ثم اعلم أن النزاع في تصور الماهية النوعية للعلم وإدراكها ليس حضوريا، وإن كان إدراك جزئياته حضوريا، فلا يرد أن العلم صفة للنفس، وإدراكها بذاتها وصفاتها حضوري، فلا يتصف بالضرورة والنظرية. وما قيل إنه من مقولة الكيف، فله جنس وفصل، فله حد، فتصوره تصور بالكنه، وهو من خصائص النظري ساقط؛ لأن ذلك ممنوع، بل كما جاز كون تنبيه القضايا الضرورية في صورة الدليل كذلك يجوز كون معرف الضروريات التصورية في صورة الحد. (نعم، تنقيح حقيقته) أي حقيقة الإدراك الحقيقي تفصيلا (عسير) تنقيح حقيقة العلم عسير جدا؛ لكمال ظهوره، بل خفائه أو اليأس عنه، ولذا اختلف فيه، فذهب علمائنا الماتريدية - عليهم الرحمة - إلى أنه حالة بسيطة قائمة بالمدرك، ذات إضافة، عليها مدار الامتياز. وقالوا: لو قام تلك الحالة بالجماد لكان شاعرا، ومدار العلم والجهل على تعلقه بالواقعيات والاختراعيات، ولا يبالون بتعلق الإضافة بالمعدومات، ولا في كونها ممتازة امتيازا علميا، فتأمل فيه. وذهب الإمام الرازي - عليه الرحمة - إلى أنه إضافة، والعقل ينقبض عنه، وذهب جمع إلى أنه مقارنة العالم للمعلوم في العقل الفعال، وهو باطل؛ لأن كل المعلومات حاصلة فيها، فنحن إما مقارنون بالكل، فيلزم أن لا يكون شيء ما مجهولا لنا، أو بالبعض، فينقسم العقل الفعال. وذهب جمع إلى أنه اتحاد مع العقل الفعال، وجمع إلى أنه اتحاد مع المعلوم. قال الشيخ وكان رجل يقال له فرفوريوس يفرط في هذا الرأي، وهما أيضا باطلان؛ لأن العاقل إذا اتحد مع المعقول أو العقل الفعال فإما بقي كما كان، فحال العلم والجهل سواء، وإما بطل عنه شيء، فالباطل إما صفته، فيكون استحالته لا اتحادا، أو ذاته، فيلزم عدم العاقل عند التعقل، وإما كمل، فإن كمل ذاته كمالا نوعيا فيكون العاقل جنسا، والعقل أو المعقول فصلا، والمجموع نوعا مركبا منهما، أو صفته فيكون كونا لها لا اتحادا. وهذا التقرير أجمع للشقوق مما في «الإشارات»، وههنا برهان آخر، سيجيئ إن شاء الله تعالى. والمشهور من الحكماء أن الصورة الحاضرة إما عين المعلوم بالذات، كما عليه الجمهور، أو شبحه المغاير بالذات، كما يفهم من ظاهر عبارات الشيخ المقتول، وهو مختار سيد العلماء، سند الأتقياء، ذي الجهاد الأكبر، المطلع على أسرار رب العزة، أبي وأستاذي نظام الملة والدين. والدليل عليه ورود إشكالات غير مندفعة على أصحاب الاتحاد بالذات بين العلم والمعلوم، كما يظهر إن شاء الله.
The dispute then with the last two groups is verbal. If He intended the intuition of the real essence of knowledge which is the referent of this concept, then the claim is theoretical, and the proof is incomplete; because we do not accept that the annexed to 'ilm al-nur' is known by essence intuitively; only the masdari meaning is known. Then know that the dispute regarding the conception of the specific quiddity of knowledge and its apprehension is not presentational, even if the apprehension of its particulars is presentational; so it is not objected that knowledge is an attribute of the soul, and its apprehension of itself and its attributes is presentational, so it is not characterized by necessity and theoreticity. And what was said that it is from the category of quality, so it has a genus and a differentia, so it has a definition, so its conception is a conception by essence, and that is from the characteristics of the theoretical — that is invalid; because that is denied, rather just as it is permissible to present necessary propositions in the form of a proof, so it is permissible to make known necessary conceptual things in the form of a definition. (Yes, refining its reality) i.e., the reality of real apprehension in detail (is difficult). Refining the reality of knowledge is very difficult; due to its perfect manifestness, rather its hiddenness or despair of it, and thus there is disagreement about it. So our Maturidi scholars — may mercy be upon them — held that it is a simple state subsisting in the perceiver, having a relation, upon which discrimination depends. And they said: If that state subsisted in an inanimate object, it would be sentient; and the basis of knowledge and ignorance is its connection to realities and inventions, and they do not care about the relation to non-existents, nor about its being distinguished by a scientific distinction — so reflect on it. And Imam al-Razi — may mercy be upon him — held that it is a relation, and the mind shrinks from it. And a group held that it is the conjunction of the knower with the known in the active intellect, and this is false; because all known things are contained in it, so we are either conjoined completely, which would necessitate that nothing be unknown to us, or partially, which would divide the active intellect. And a group held that it is union with the active intellect, and another group held that it is union with the known. The Shaykh said: There was a man called Porphyry who exaggerated this view, and both are also false; because if the knower unites with the intelligible or the active intellect, then either he remains as he was — so the state of knowledge and ignorance is the same — or something is lost from him, so what is lost is either his attribute, so that is a change of state not a union, or his essence, which necessitates the non-existence of the knower at the time of intellection, or something is completed; if his essence is completed by a specific perfection, then the knower is a genus and the intellect or the intelligible is a differentia, and the composite is a species composed of them, or if his attribute is completed, then it is a coming into being for it, not a union. And this exposition is more comprehensive of the divisions than what is in the 'Isharat', and here is another proof, which will come, God willing. And the well-known among the philosophers is that the present form is either the very object known in essence, as the majority hold, or its phantom distinct in essence, as is understood from the apparent expressions of the martyred Shaykh, and it is the chosen view of the master of scholars, the support of the pious, the one of the greater jihad, the one who is informed of the secrets of the Lord of Might, my father and teacher, Nizam al-Milla wa al-Din. And the evidence for it is the occurrence of irrefutable objections against the proponents of essential union between knowledge and the known, as will appear, God willing.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the difficulty of defining the reality of knowledge. It opens by clarifying that any dispute with the last two groups is merely verbal, then addresses whether the essence of real knowledge is intuitively known or theoretical. The author argues that the quiddity of knowledge is not known by presence (huduri) since it is a specific type, and refutes the claim that it must be definitional simply because it belongs to the category of quality. He then quotes the matn: 'Yes, refining its reality is difficult' and explains that the reality of knowledge is hard to refine due to its extreme manifestness or hiddenness, leading to disagreement. He presents the Maturidi position: knowledge is a simple state (hala basita) subsisting in the perceiver, with an attribution (idafa) that is the basis for discrimination. They hold it would make an inanimate object sentient, and its relation to realities and inventions distinguishes knowledge from ignorance. He notes that they do not consider relation to non-existents or scientific distinction. Then he presents Imam al-Razi's view that it is a relation (idafa), with the mind shrinking from it. Another group held it is conjunction (muqarana) with the active intellect, which is refuted: if we are completely conjoined, nothing would be unknown; if partially, the active intellect divides. Another group held union (ittihad) with the active intellect, and another with the known. The Shaykh mentions Porphyry's exaggeration. Both union views are refuted through a disjunctive argument: after union, either the knower remains unchanged (no difference), or something is lost (either attribute, so change not union, or essence, so annihilation), or something is completed (either essence, making knower genus and intellect differentia, or attribute, making it a coming into being). This refutation is more comprehensive than Avicenna's in the 'Isharat'. Finally, he mentions the well-known philosophical view that the present form is either the known object itself or its phantom, and the latter is preferred by his teacher, supported by objections against identity theory. All examples used are from the Islamic philosophical tradition (Maturidi kalam, peripatetic philosophy, etc.).

Key Terms
العلم
الحالة البسيطة
الإضافة
الاتحاد
المقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi definition presented in this passage, what is the nature of knowledge?
A) It is a relation (idafa) between the knower and the known.
B) It is a simple state (hala basita) subsisting in the perceiver, having a relation.
C) It is conjunction (muqarana) with the active intellect.
D) It is union (ittihad) with the known object.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi definition identifies knowledge as a simple state with a relation; union theories are refuted through an exhaustive case analysis showing each outcome is either impossible or not a true union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Unity of Knowledge: A Dialectical Defense
Author argues knowledge is one reality and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's conditional argument for the unity of knowledge and identify the dialectical moves (claim, objection, response).

Core Idea
Knowledge is one reality; otherwise concept and assent could not be species under it.

In this passage, the author defends the claim that knowledge is a single reality common to all particular sciences. He presents a conditional argument and then answers an objection from those who say the essence of knowledge is unknown. This lesson breaks down the logical structure and dialectical flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the Arabic sentence where the author asserts that knowledge is a single reality common to all sciences.
The phrase 'العلم حقيقة واحدة مشتركة' is key.
2
Extract the conditional argument
Locate the conditional statement: if knowledge were not one reality, then concept and assent could not be two types under it. Identify the antecedent and consequent.
The Arabic: 'لو لم يكن حقيقة واحدة لما صح جعل التصور والتصديق نوعين تحته'.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector asks how this argument can convince someone who believes the reality of knowledge is unknown. Identify the doubt being raised.
The objection: 'من يقول بمجهولية حقيقة العلم كيف يقتنع بهذا؟'
4
Examine the response
The author replies by arguing that diversity without a common essence is impossible, even if the essence is unknown. He uses an analogy with human beings and warns of absurd consequences.
The response uses analogy and reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نعلم قطعا أن العلم حقيقة واحدة مشتركة بين العلوم الخاصة اشتراكا معنويا ، وإن كان هذا عسى أن يكون مكابرة. وأيضا لو لم يكن حقيقة واحدة لما صح جعل التصور والتصديق نوعين تحته. فإن قلت : من يقول بمجهولية حقيقة العلم كيف يقنع بهذا ؟ قلت: اختلاف العلوم بحيث لا يكون ذاتي مشترك بينها ضروري الاستحالة، وإن كانت مجهولة الكنه، كما أن اختلاف الأناسي بالحقيقة ضروري الاستحالة ، وإن كان كنهها مجهولا ، وإبداء مثل هذه الاحتمالات يبطل اتحاد نوعي بين كل شخصين. وعلى هذين الجوابين يلزم أن يكون العلم دعوی بالجوهر شيئا ، وبالكم شيئا آخر ، وهكذا هذا
Then we know with certainty that knowledge is a single reality common to particular sciences with a meaningful commonality, even if this might be an exaggeration. Also, if it were not a single reality, it would not be valid to make concept and assent as two types under it. If you say: the one who says that the reality of knowledge is unknown, how can he be satisfied with this? I say: The diversity of sciences such that there is no common essential among them is necessarily impossible, even if their essence is unknown, just as the diversity of human beings in reality is necessarily impossible, even if their essence is unknown, and presenting such possibilities invalidates the specific unity between every two persons. And according to these two answers, it follows that knowledge would be one thing regarding substance and another thing regarding quantity, and so on.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending the unity of knowledge as a single reality. The author begins by asserting that we know with certainty that knowledge is a single reality common to all particular sciences, and that this is a meaningful (not homonymous) commonality. He then gives a logical argument: if knowledge were not one reality, it would be impossible to classify concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) as two types under it. This is a conditional inference—if the genus is not one, the species cannot fall under it. Next, an objector questions how this can convince someone who holds that the reality of knowledge is unknown. The author responds by showing that diversity without a shared essential property is impossible, even if the essence is unknown. He draws an analogy with human beings: even if the essence of humanity is unknown, it is impossible for humans to have no common reality. Then he points out that accepting such possibilities would lead to absurdities, like making knowledge different for substance and quantity. The argument is structured as a claim, objection, and refutation.

التصور والتصديق

Key Takeaway
The unity of knowledge is defended by a conditional argument that if knowledge were not one reality, the division into concept and assent would be impossible.

The Division of the Perceptual State into Concept and Assent
Author explains how image becomes knowledge and divides the state into tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq and explain why the image alone is not knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not the image but the perceptual state that unites with it.

This passage clarifies a fundamental distinction in Islamic logic: the difference between merely having an image in the mind and actually knowing something. The author shows that an image becomes knowledge only when a perceptual state mixes with it in a relational, unifying way. This state then divides into two kinds: simple apprehension (tasawwur) and judgment (tasdiq). Understanding this division is key to grasping how the mind works in acquiring knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: why the image alone is not knowledge
The author states that the image is not knowledge in itself because knowledge is a single reality. Recognise that an image, like a mental picture, lacks the quality of 'knowledge' until something else is added.
2
Understand how the perceptual state transforms the image
The perceptual state mixes with the image's impressed existence in the mind. This mixing is not additive but relational and unifying—like the attribute 'writer' uniting with the person. Notice that this state is abstracted from the image and subsists in the mind alongside it.
The example of 'writer' and 'human' helps illustrate the unifying nature.
3
Recognise that the perceptual state is accidental and sense-specific
The author explains that this state is not essential to the image; otherwise, external images would always be accompanied by it. It is an accident that occurs only in the mind or sense organ, and it varies by sense (taste, hearing, etc.).
This shows that the same image can be known in different modalities.
4
Grasp the final division into tasawwur and tasdiq
The perceptual state is divided into two types: tasawwur (simple concept, like understanding 'human') and tasdiq (assent/judgment, like affirming 'human is rational'). Observe that this division is exhaustive—every act of knowledge falls into one of these categories.
Assent (tasdiq) is defined as affirmation (idh'an), which requires a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم العلم إلى تصور وتصديق (ثُمَّ بَعْدَ التَّفْتِيشِ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ تِلْكَ الصُورَةَ) ليست علما في ذاتها ؛ لأن العلم حقيقة واحدة، والصورة ليست كذلك ، بل (إِنَّمَا صَارَتْ) تلك الصورة ( عِلْمًا ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَالَةَ الْإِدْرَاكِيَّةَ قَدْ خَالَطَتْ بِوُجُودِهَا الاِنْطِبَاعِيَّ)، أي بوجودها المنطبع الذهن، وليس خلطا انضماميا حتى يلزم كون الصورة عالمة، أو كقيام عرضين بموضوع؛ حتى يلزم ما يلزم على شارح «التجريد»، بل(۱) (خَلْطَا رَابِطِيَّا اتِّحَادِيَّا ، كخلط الكاتب بالإنسان، وتلك منتزعة من الصورة متحدة معها في الوجود، قائمة بالذهن ؛ فإن أحد المتحدين إذا قام بالموضوع وجب قيام المتحد الآخر بالضرورة ، كما صرح به الشيخ في «قاطيغورياس الشفاء». فإن قلت: فكيف يكون العلم كيفا ؟ . :قلت كونها انتزاعية لا ينافي الكيفية، كالكيفيات التي في الكميات . ثم إن تلك الحالة ليست لازمة للصورة، ولا ذاتية لها، وإلا لما وجدت في الخارج معراة عنها، بل من الأعراض التي لا تعرض إلا في الذهن أو الحاسة، وهي تنقسم إلى أنواعها من الذوق والشم وغيرهما، كما أشار إليه، (كَالْحَالَةِ الذَّوْقِيَّةِ)، أي الحالة الإدراكية التي في القوة الذائقة (بِالْمَذُوقَاتِ)، أي الكيفية المذوقة، وهي أنواع الطعم حين حصولها في الذائقة، (فَصَارَتْ صُورَةً ذَوْقِيَّةٌ ، وَالسَّمْعِيَّةُ بِالْمَسْمُوعَاتِ)، وهي الأصوات حين حصولها في السامعة ، (وَهَكَذَا) ، كالشمية بالمشمومات ، وهي الروائح، والتوهم بالوهميات حين حصولها في التوهم، والتخيل بالجزئيات الحاصلة في الحس المشترك ، والتعقل بالكليات الحاصلة في العقل. (وَتِلْكَ الْحَالَةُ تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى التَّصَوُّرِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِ)، الذي هو الإذعان
Division of knowledge into concept and assent. Then after examination it is known that that image is not knowledge in itself, because knowledge is a single reality, and the image is not like that. Rather, that image only becomes knowledge because the perceptual state has mixed with its impression existence, i.e., with its impressed existence in the mind, and it is not an additive mixture so that the image would be knowing, nor like the subsistence of two accidents on a subject so that what follows for the commentator of al-Tajrid would follow. Rather, it is a relational unifying mixture, like the mixture of the writer with the human, and that [state] is abstracted from the image, united with it in existence, subsisting in the mind. For if one of the unities subsists in the subject, the subsistence of the other necessarily follows, as the shaykh stated in the Categories of al-Shifa. If you say: how then is knowledge a quality? I say: its being abstractive does not contradict quality, like the qualities in quantities. Then that state is not necessary for the image nor essential to it, otherwise it would not exist in the external stripped of it. Rather, it is among the accidents that only occur in the mind or the sense organ, and it divides into its types like taste, smell, and others, as he indicated, like the taste state, i.e., the perceptual state in the power of tasting with regard to tasted things, i.e., the tasted quality, which are the types of taste when they occur in the tasting power, so it becomes a taste image. And the auditory state is with sounds, i.e., sounds when they occur in the hearing power, and so on, like the olfactory state with smelled things, i.e., odors, and the estimative state with estimable things when they occur in estimation, and the imaginative state with particulars occurring in the common sense, and the rational state with universals occurring in the intellect. And that state divides into concept and assent, which is affirmation.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of how the scientific image becomes knowledge and then divides the perceptual state into concept and assent. The author begins by noting that after examination, it becomes clear that the image is not knowledge in itself, because knowledge is a single reality, whereas the image is not. Rather, the image becomes knowledge because the perceptual state mixes with its existence in the mind. This mixing is not additive (like adding two things together) but is a relational unifying mixture, analogous to the mixture of 'writer' with 'human'—the writer attribute is abstracted from the human but united with him in existence. The author cites the shaykh (Avicenna) in the Categories of al-Shifa for the principle that if one of two unities subsists in a subject, the other necessarily subsists. Then an objection arises: If knowledge is this state, how can it be a quality (kayf)? The response is that this state is abstractive (i'itibari), which does not contradict quality; just as there are qualities in quantities. The state is not essential to the image; otherwise images would not exist in the external world without it. It is an accident that occurs only in the mind or sense organ. This state has various types corresponding to different senses: taste, hearing, smell, estimation, imagination, and intellection. Finally, this perceptual state is divided into two kinds: concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), where assent is affirmation (idh'an).

Key Terms
الحالة الإدراكية
التصور
التصديق
الانطباع
الخلط الرابطي الاتحادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In an additive, compositional manner
B) In a relational, unifying manner
C) In a manner that makes the image itself a knower
D) In a manner that makes the state essential to the image

Key Takeaway
The perceptual state that makes an image into knowledge is an accidental, relational unity that divides into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

?

Distinguishing Badihi and Nazari in Knowledge
Author states and comments on the division of tasawwur and tasdiq into badihi and nazari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given piece of knowledge (tasawwur or tasdiq) as badihi or nazari using the criteria from the text.

Core Idea
Knowledge divides into self-evident and theoretical; but self-evidence can be relative to description.

In this passage, the author explains a fundamental division: knowledge is either badihi (self-evident, no reflection needed) or nazari (requires proof). This division applies to both concepts (tasawwur) and affirmations (tasdiq). However, the author adds nuance: a proposition can be self-evident under one description and theoretical under another. Understanding this division is crucial for building sound arguments without infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Define badihi and nazari
Write definitions based on the text. For badihi in tasdiq, note that it requires the judgement not to depend on reflection after conceptualizing the extremes.
2
Apply the definition to an example
Take the proposition 'The Quran is the word of God'. Consider it as used in a kalam argument for prophecy. Is it badihi or nazari? Why?
Think about whether one needs reflection after conceptualizing 'Quran' and 'word of God'.
3
Consider the effect of description
The author says the same proposition can be badihi under one description and nazari under another. For example, 'The Quran is the word of God' is badihi for someone who hears it and recognizes its inimitability, but nazari for someone who needs to analyze its linguistic miracle.
This shows that classification is not inherent to the proposition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَبَعْض كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ (1) (۱) البداهة في التصور واضحة وأما في التصديق فمعناها أن يكون الحكم بعد تصور الطرفين غير متوقف على نظر، سواء كان تصور أحد طرفيه أو كليهما نظريا أو بديهيا ، عند الحكماء القائلين بأن التصديق هو الحكم ، وأنه بسيط، وأما على أنه مركب فلا يكون التصديق . بديهيا إلا إذا كان جميع أجزاء المركب بديهيا كما سبقت مني الإشارة إليه، فافهم فإنه مهم راجع السيد على شرح الشمسية : ٩۳/۱ ، ٩٤ ، الدسوقي على شرح الخبيصي: ٥٢ ، ٥٣ . التقسيم إلى ضروري ونظري (۲) (۳) : لا بد من دعوى وَبَعْضُهُ (۱) نَظْرِيٌّ) ، نتيجة للدليل (٢) . قال المحقق البديهة في مقدمات الدليل وأطرافها ؛ حذرا عن لزوم التسلسل، وهو يؤول إلى دعوى البديهة في المطلوب، فليكتف به ..أولا ولا يخفى عليك أن البديهة والنظرية يختلفان باختلاف العنوان فرب قضية إذا عبر موضوعها بعنوان تصير نظرية، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر تصير بديهية، فيجوز أن يكون إذا لوحظ إلى المقدمات أنفسها يحكم ببداهتها بديهة، وإذا لوحظ بعنوان بعض التصديقات والتصورات يكون بداهتها نظرية، فلا أول . نعم، لو استدل أولا بأن هذه المقدمات وأطرافها ضرورية، فبعض التصورات أو التصديقات ضرورة لكفى (۱) كذا في السلم، وشرح ملا حسن ليس فيه: «وبعضه»، بينما الذي في شرح بحر العلوم: فبعض من كل منهما بديهي وبعضه نظري». (۲) وهذا الاستنتاج من دليله - وهو وليس الكل من كل إلخ - مبني على أمرين، الأول: أن كل واحد من البديهي والنظري موضوع لمعنى واحد مشترك بين التصور والتصديق، والثاني عدم الواسطة بينهما وإلا لما لزم من نفيهما الانقسام راجع السعدية: ١٠٥، ١٠٦ ، عبد الحكيم على شرح القطب على الشمسية: ٠٩١/١ (۳) في شرحه على تهذيب المنطق (٤) (ب) حيث استحسن صنيع السعد في التهذيب إذ قال ويقتسمان بالضرورة الضرورة والاكتساب بالنظر ، وما ذكره الشارح هنا حاصل كلامه، وأما نص عبارته فهو: «وهذا الطريق، أعني الإحالة إلى البداهة أسلم من الاستدلال عليه بأنه لو كان الكلُّ من كل منهما نظريا لدار أو تسلسل، أو بديهيا لما احتجنا في شيء منهما إلى الفكر ؛ فإنه مع ما فيه من التوقف على امتناع اكتساب التصديق من التصور ، ثم على حدوث النفس على ما هو المشهور لا يتم إلا بدعوى البديهة في مقدمات الدليل وأطرافها، وذلك كافٍ في نفي كسبية الكل، فلا حاجة إلى الدليل عليه، لا بد من دعوى البديهة في ثبوت الاحتياج إلى الفكر، وذلك بعينه دعوى البديهة في عدم بداهة الكل، فظهر أن الاستدلال يؤول بالآخرة إلى دعوى البديهة في المطلوب، فَلْيُكْتَف به أولا ، فافهم ذلك ؛ فإنه مما لا تجده لغيرنا وانظمه في سلك نظائره المنثورة هذه الحواشي». وقد تعرضنا لهذه النقطة في قسم الدراسة سابقا.
So some of each of them is necessary (1). (1) Self-evidence in tasawwur is clear, but in tasdiq its meaning is that the judgement after conceptualizing the two extremes is not dependent on reflection, whether the conceptualization of one or both extremes is theoretical or necessary, according to the philosophers who hold that tasdiq is the judgement and that it is simple. But if it is composite, then tasdiq is not self-evident unless all parts of the composite are self-evident, as I previously indicated. Understand, for it is important. Refer to [marginalia]. The division into necessary and theoretical. (2) (3): It is necessary to claim that some of it is theoretical, as a result of the proof (2). The investigator said: self-evidence in the premises of the proof and their extremes, as a precaution against infinite regress, and it ultimately amounts to claiming self-evidence in the desired conclusion, so it suffices first. And it is not hidden from you that the necessary and theoretical differ according to different descriptions. For perhaps a proposition, if its subject is expressed with a description, becomes theoretical, and if expressed with another description, becomes necessary. So it is possible that when one looks at the premises themselves, one judges their necessity self-evidently, and when one looks at them under the description of being some of the affirmations or concepts, their necessity becomes theoretical. So there is no first. Yes, if one first argues that these premises and their extremes are necessary, then some concepts or affirmations are necessary, it suffices. (1) As in al-Sullam; the commentary of Mulla Hasan does not have 'and some of it', while in Sharh Bahr al-'Ulum: 'so some of each of them is necessary and some of it is theoretical'. (2) And this conclusion from his proof—that not all from each etc.—is based on two matters: first, that each of necessary and theoretical is a subject for a single meaning common between tasawwur and tasdiq; second, the absence of an intermediary between them; otherwise the division would not follow from negating both. Refer to al-Sa'diyya: 105, 106, 'Abd al-Hakim on Sharh al-Qutb on al-Shamsiyya: 1/091. (3) In his commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq (4)(b) where he approves of al-Sa'd's practice in al-Tahdhib when he said: 'They divide by necessity into necessity and acquisition by reflection.' And what the commentator mentioned here is the summary of his speech. As for the text of his statement: 'This way, I mean referring to self-evidence, is safer than arguing that if all from each were theoretical it would lead to circularity or infinite regress, or if it were necessary we would not need thought in any of it; for it, despite its dependence on the impossibility of acquiring tasdiq from tasawwur, and then on the origination of the soul according to what is well-known, is not complete except by claiming self-evidence in the premises of the proof and their extremes, and that suffices to negate the acquiredness of all. So there is no need for the proof for it. It is necessary to claim self-evidence in establishing the need for thought, and that is the very claim of self-evidence in the non-necessity of all. So it becomes clear that the argument ultimately amounts to claiming self-evidence in the desired conclusion. So let it suffice first. Understand that, for it is something you will not find from others, and include it in the series of its scattered counterparts in these glosses.' And we have discussed this point earlier in the study section.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expansionary commentary on the division of tasawwur and tasdiq into necessary (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). The author begins by quoting the matn: 'So some of each of them is necessary.' He then clarifies the meaning of badaha in tasdiq: it is a judgement that does not depend on reflection after conceptualizing the subject and predicate, regardless of whether the conceptualization is itself necessary or theoretical. This applies according to those who consider tasdiq as a simple judgement. If tasdiq is composite, then it is self-evident only if all its parts are self-evident. Next, he introduces the division (tagsim) into necessary and theoretical. He explains that the investigator (muhaqqiq) holds that self-evidence must be claimed in the premises and their terms to avoid infinite regress (tasalsul). This ultimately reduces to claiming self-evidence in the desired conclusion itself. The author then notes that the classification into necessary and theoretical can vary depending on how the subject is described. The same proposition may be necessary under one description and theoretical under another. Therefore, there is no absolute 'first' premise that is always self-evident. However, if one starts by arguing that the premises and their terms are necessary, then some concepts or affirmations are necessary, and that suffices. The passage also includes extensive footnote material, quoting from al-Sullam, Mulla Hasan, al-Sa'diyya, and others, which the commentary can reference but the main explanation should follow the primary text.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into badihi and nazari, but the classification is not absolute; it depends on the description of the subject.

Infinite Regress and the Primacy of Self-Evidence
Author states and comments on the division of tasawwur and tasdiq into badihi and nazari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why self-evident premises are necessary to avoid infinite regress and how the relativity of description affects the classification of knowledge.

Core Idea
To avoid infinite regress, we must accept some knowledge as self-evident; but what is self-evident can depend on how we describe it.

The author explains that if all knowledge required proof, we would face an infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, we must claim self-evidence (badaha) in at least some premises. However, he adds that the necessity of a proposition can vary according to its description. This insight prevents dogmatism and highlights the pragmatic foundation of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the regress problem
Why does the author mention infinite regress? What would happen if all knowledge were nazari?
2
Find the solution proposed
The investigator says: self-evidence in the premises and their terms suffices. How does that stop the regress?
3
Examine the description relativity
The author gives an example: a proposition can be theoretical under one description and necessary under another. Apply this to an Islamic proof: 'The Quran is inimitable' is badihi if you experience it directly, but nazari if you need to analyze its style.
4
Understand the meta-claim
The argument ultimately reduces to claiming self-evidence in the desired conclusion. Why does the author say 'so it suffices first'? What is the benefit of this approach?

فَبَعْض كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ (1) (۱) البداهة في التصور واضحة وأما في التصديق فمعناها أن يكون الحكم بعد تصور الطرفين غير متوقف على نظر، سواء كان تصور أحد طرفيه أو كليهما نظريا أو بديهيا ، عند الحكماء القائلين بأن التصديق هو الحكم ، وأنه بسيط، وأما على أنه مركب فلا يكون التصديق . بديهيا إلا إذا كان جميع أجزاء المركب بديهيا كما سبقت مني الإشارة إليه، فافهم فإنه مهم راجع السيد على شرح الشمسية : ٩۳/۱ ، ٩٤ ، الدسوقي على شرح الخبيصي: ٥٢ ، ٥٣ . التقسيم إلى ضروري ونظري (۲) (۳) : لا بد من دعوى وَبَعْضُهُ (۱) نَظْرِيٌّ) ، نتيجة للدليل (٢) . قال المحقق البديهة في مقدمات الدليل وأطرافها ؛ حذرا عن لزوم التسلسل، وهو يؤول إلى دعوى البديهة في المطلوب، فليكتف به ..أولا ولا يخفى عليك أن البديهة والنظرية يختلفان باختلاف العنوان فرب قضية إذا عبر موضوعها بعنوان تصير نظرية، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر تصير بديهية، فيجوز أن يكون إذا لوحظ إلى المقدمات أنفسها يحكم ببداهتها بديهة، وإذا لوحظ بعنوان بعض التصديقات والتصورات يكون بداهتها نظرية، فلا أول . نعم، لو استدل أولا بأن هذه المقدمات وأطرافها ضرورية، فبعض التصورات أو التصديقات ضرورة لكفى (۱) كذا في السلم، وشرح ملا حسن ليس فيه: «وبعضه»، بينما الذي في شرح بحر العلوم: فبعض من كل منهما بديهي وبعضه نظري». (۲) وهذا الاستنتاج من دليله - وهو وليس الكل من كل إلخ - مبني على أمرين، الأول: أن كل واحد من البديهي والنظري موضوع لمعنى واحد مشترك بين التصور والتصديق، والثاني عدم الواسطة بينهما وإلا لما لزم من نفيهما الانقسام راجع السعدية: ١٠٥، ١٠٦ ، عبد الحكيم على شرح القطب على الشمسية: ٠٩١/١ (۳) في شرحه على تهذيب المنطق (٤) (ب) حيث استحسن صنيع السعد في التهذيب إذ قال ويقتسمان بالضرورة الضرورة والاكتساب بالنظر ، وما ذكره الشارح هنا حاصل كلامه، وأما نص عبارته فهو: «وهذا الطريق، أعني الإحالة إلى البداهة أسلم من الاستدلال عليه بأنه لو كان الكلُّ من كل منهما نظريا لدار أو تسلسل، أو بديهيا لما احتجنا في شيء منهما إلى الفكر ؛ فإنه مع ما فيه من التوقف على امتناع اكتساب التصديق من التصور ، ثم على حدوث النفس على ما هو المشهور لا يتم إلا بدعوى البديهة في مقدمات الدليل وأطرافها، وذلك كافٍ في نفي كسبية الكل، فلا حاجة إلى الدليل عليه، لا بد من دعوى البديهة في ثبوت الاحتياج إلى الفكر، وذلك بعينه دعوى البديهة في عدم بداهة الكل، فظهر أن الاستدلال يؤول بالآخرة إلى دعوى البديهة في المطلوب، فَلْيُكْتَف به أولا ، فافهم ذلك ؛ فإنه مما لا تجده لغيرنا وانظمه في سلك نظائره المنثورة هذه الحواشي». وقد تعرضنا لهذه النقطة في قسم الدراسة سابقا.

Definition of Logic as an Instrumental Law
Author defines logic as a law preventing error in reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and definition type (hadd/rasm) in the definition of logic and assess its completeness.

Core Idea
Logic is a law that safeguards the mind from error in the arrangement of thoughts.

In this passage, the author defines logic as an instrumental law that prevents error in the arrangement of thoughts. This definition distinguishes logic from mere natural reasoning and from empirical knowledge. We will dissect this definition to understand its components and why it is considered a complete description (rasm tamm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. Here it is 'al-mantiq' (logic).
2
Extract the genus
What broader category is logic placed in? The text says it is a 'qanun' (law) or 'aala qanooniyya' (instrumental law). So the genus is 'instrumental law'.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes logic from other laws? The differentia is 'that prevents error in the arrangement of thoughts' ('tasim mara'ataha al-dhahn 'an al-khata' fi al-fikr').
4
Determine the definition type
Is this a hadd (real definition) or rasm (description)? It is a rasm because it describes logic by its function (safeguarding from error) rather than its essence. It is a complete rasm because it captures both the purpose and the domain.
The author says 'rasamuhu' (they described it), indicating rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ تَرْتِيبٍ مُفِيدًا، بأن يخطئ في المادة، ولا طبعيًا بأن يخطئ في الصورة، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ تَرَى الْآرَاءَ، أي آراء الأجلة الطالبين للصواب، الهاربين عن الخطأ مُتَنَاقِضَةً، بل رُأي واحد يناقض نفسه في زمانين، والفطرة الإنسانية غير كافية، وإلا لما وقع كثيرا. فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ قَانُونِ عَاصِمٍ عَنْ الْخَطَأَ فِيهِ، أي في الترتيب إذا روعي، وَهُوَ أي القانون العاصم الْمَنْطِقُ. ورسموه بأنه آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر. وههنا أبحاث، الأول: أن الخطأ في الأفكار الجزئية لا يحوج إلا إلى ما، سواء كان معرفة طريق جزئي، أو قانون كلي، فحينئذ ثبت الاحتياج إلى الأعم من المنطق، لا إليه. والجواب: بأن العلم اليقيني بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا بالكليات، فمعرفة الطرق الجزئية يستحيل إلا من الكليات التي هي القانون، فثبت الحاجة إليه، وبأن معرفة الطرق الجزئية بأشخاصها لعدم تناهيها من المحالات، فلا بد من قانون غير تام؛ لأن استحالة بعض خصوصيات المحتاج إليه لا يوجب إلى البعض الممكن .
This, and not every arrangement is beneficial – such as error in matter, nor natural – such as error in form. And hence you see opinions, i.e., opinions of eminent seekers of truth, fleeing from error, contradictory, rather a single opinion contradicts itself in two times, and human nature is insufficient, otherwise error would not occur frequently. So there must be a law that prevents error in it, i.e., in arrangement when observed, and it, i.e., the preventing law, is logic. And they defined it as an instrumental law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought. And here there are investigations: the first: that error in particular thoughts only necessitates something, whether it is knowledge of a particular method or a universal law, so then the need for the more general than logic is established, not for logic itself. And the answer: that certain knowledge of particulars is only obtained through universals, so knowledge of particular methods is impossible except from the universals which are the law, so the need for it is established; and because knowledge of particular methods individually is impossible due to their infinitude, so there must be a law, albeit incomplete; for the impossibility of some specific needed things does not necessitate [the impossibility of] the possible ones.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines logic and argues for its necessity. It begins by noting that not every arrangement of thought is beneficial; error can occur in matter (content) or in form (structure). Human nature alone is insufficient, as evidenced by contradictions among thinkers. Therefore, a law (qanun) is needed to protect the mind from error in arranging thoughts. This law is logic, defined as an instrumental law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought. The text then addresses an objection: if error occurs only in particular thoughts, perhaps particular methods suffice without a universal law. The answer has two parts: first, certain knowledge of particulars requires universals, so particular methods are impossible without universal laws (i.e., logic). Second, since particular methods are infinite, only a universal law can cover them all, even if that law is not perfect (ghayr tam). The argument thus establishes the necessity of logic despite the possibility of partial insufficiency.

قانون عاصم
آلة قانونية
الخطأ في الفكر

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic as an instrumental law that safeguards the mind from error in thought is a complete rasm, specifying its function and domain.

The Necessity of Logic: Why Human Reason Needs a Law
Author argues for the necessity of logic by answering an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against the need for logic and the author's response, and explain why universal laws are necessary for certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Even particular knowledge requires universal laws; infinite particular methods are impossible without logic.

After defining logic, the author anticipates an objection: if error occurs in particular thoughts, perhaps particular methods suffice without a universal law. The author gives a twofold response, showing that certain knowledge of particulars depends on universals and that particular methods are infinite. This argument solidifies the necessity of logic as an indispensable tool for sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Find the objector's claim: 'error in particular thoughts only needs something, whether it is knowledge of a particular method or a universal law, so need for the more general than logic is established, not for logic itself.'
The objector thinks particular methods are sufficient, making logic unnecessary.
2
Find the first part of the answer
The author responds: 'certain knowledge of particulars is only obtained through universals, so knowledge of particular methods is impossible except from the universals which are the law.' This means that to know a particular method with certainty, you must already have universal principles.
This is an epistemological argument: certainty requires universals.
3
Find the second part of the answer
The author adds: 'knowledge of particular methods individually is impossible due to their infinitude, so there must be a law, albeit incomplete.' Since there are infinitely many possible particular methods, we need a universal law to cover them all.
The law may not be perfect (ghayr tamm), but it is still necessary.
4
Synthesize the author's conclusion
The objection fails because (1) certainty in particulars requires universals, and (2) the infinite number of particulars necessitates a universal covering law. Hence logic is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ تَرْتِيبٍ مُفِيدًا، بأن يخطئ في المادة، ولا طبعيًا بأن يخطئ في الصورة، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ تَرَى الْآرَاءَ، أي آراء الأجلة الطالبين للصواب، الهاربين عن الخطأ مُتَنَاقِضَةً، بل رُأي واحد يناقض نفسه في زمانين، والفطرة الإنسانية غير كافية، وإلا لما وقع كثيرا. فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ قَانُونِ عَاصِمٍ عَنْ الْخَطَأَ فِيهِ، أي في الترتيب إذا روعي، وَهُوَ أي القانون العاصم الْمَنْطِقُ. ورسموه بأنه آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر. وههنا أبحاث، الأول: أن الخطأ في الأفكار الجزئية لا يحوج إلا إلى ما، سواء كان معرفة طريق جزئي، أو قانون كلي، فحينئذ ثبت الاحتياج إلى الأعم من المنطق، لا إليه. والجواب: بأن العلم اليقيني بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا بالكليات، فمعرفة الطرق الجزئية يستحيل إلا من الكليات التي هي القانون، فثبت الحاجة إليه، وبأن معرفة الطرق الجزئية بأشخاصها لعدم تناهيها من المحالات، فلا بد من قانون غير تام؛ لأن استحالة بعض خصوصيات المحتاج إليه لا يوجب إلى البعض الممكن .
This, and not every arrangement is beneficial – such as error in matter, nor natural – such as error in form. And hence you see opinions, i.e., opinions of eminent seekers of truth, fleeing from error, contradictory, rather a single opinion contradicts itself in two times, and human nature is insufficient, otherwise error would not occur frequently. So there must be a law that prevents error in it, i.e., in arrangement when observed, and it, i.e., the preventing law, is logic. And they defined it as an instrumental law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought. And here there are investigations: the first: that error in particular thoughts only necessitates something, whether it is knowledge of a particular method or a universal law, so then the need for the more general than logic is established, not for logic itself. And the answer: that certain knowledge of particulars is only obtained through universals, so knowledge of particular methods is impossible except from the universals which are the law, so the need for it is established; and because knowledge of particular methods individually is impossible due to their infinitude, so there must be a law, albeit incomplete; for the impossibility of some specific needed things does not necessitate [the impossibility of] the possible ones.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines logic and argues for its necessity. It begins by noting that not every arrangement of thought is beneficial; error can occur in matter (content) or in form (structure). Human nature alone is insufficient, as evidenced by contradictions among thinkers. Therefore, a law (qanun) is needed to protect the mind from error in arranging thoughts. This law is logic, defined as an instrumental law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought. The text then addresses an objection: if error occurs only in particular thoughts, perhaps particular methods suffice without a universal law. The answer has two parts: first, certain knowledge of particulars requires universals, so particular methods are impossible without universal laws (i.e., logic). Second, since particular methods are infinite, only a universal law can cover them all, even if that law is not perfect (ghayr tam). The argument thus establishes the necessity of logic despite the possibility of partial insufficiency.

Key Terms
الأفكار الجزئية
الطرق الجزئية
القانون الكلي
العلم اليقيني
عدم التناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is knowledge of particular methods impossible without universal laws?
A) Because particular methods are infinite.
B) Because certain knowledge of particulars requires universals.
C) Because universal laws are easier to learn.
D) Because logic is the most general science.

Key Takeaway
Logic is necessary because both epistemological and pragmatic reasons show that universal laws are indispensable for certain and comprehensive knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
An opponent argues: 'To avoid error in fiqh, we only need to memorize the rulings of specific cases, not universal principles of analogy.' Using the author's first argument, show why universal principles are still necessary for certain knowledge.
?
Imagine a student says: 'There are too many logical formulas to learn; I'll just rely on my natural reasoning.' How would the author's second argument (infinitude of particular methods) refute this? Write a short response.

Definition of Logic as a Law Preventing Error and Need for It
Author defines logic by its purpose and answers objection on necessity of a universal law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of logic as a protecting law and defend its necessity against the objection that particular knowledge suffices.

Core Idea
Logic is the law that protects the mind, and its necessity is proven by the dependence of certain knowledge on universals.

In this passage, the author defines logic by its purpose (bi al-rasm) as a legal instrument that safeguards the mind from error. He then addresses a crucial objection: why do we need a universal law of logic when errors in particular thoughts could be corrected with particular methods? The response reveals a foundational principle: certain knowledge (al-yaqīn al-dā’im) of particulars is impossible without universal principles, making logic indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of logic
Find where the author explicitly defines logic. Look for the phrase 'وهو المنطق' and the description 'آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر'. Note that this is a definition by description (rasm), not by genus and differentia.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that errors in particular thoughts can be addressed without a universal law—only particular methods are needed. Locate the sentence starting with 'أن الخطأ في الأفكار الجزئية لا يحوج إلا إلى ما'.
3
Analyze the first response
The author argues that certain knowledge of particulars depends on universals. Find the claim 'العلم اليقيني بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا بالكليات'. This is the key epistemic principle.
4
Examine the response about infinity
The author adds that particular cases are infinite, so a universal law is necessary. The analogy with corporeal form illustrates that impossibility of some aspects does not negate need for possible ones.
5
Connect to the purpose of demonstration
The ultimate goal is perpetual certainty (al-yaqīn al-dā’im), which requires universal principles. Thus, the need for logic is real.

وههنا أبحاث، الأولن أن الخطأ في الأفكار الجزئية لا يحوج إلا إلى ما، سواء كان معرفة طريق جزئي، أو قانون كلي، فحينئذ ثبت الاحتياج إلى الأعم من المنطق ، لا إليه.
والجواب: بأن العلم اليقيني بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا بالكليات، فمعرفة الطرق الجزئية يستحيل إلا من الكليات التي هي القانون، فثبت الحاجة إليه، وبأن معرفة الطرق الجزئية بأشخاصها لعدم تناهيها من المحالات ، فلا بد من قانون غير تام؛ لأن استحالة بعض خصوصيات المحتاج إليه لا يوجب إلى البعض الممكن .
ألا تری أن الاستحالة الصورة (۱) الجسمية الغير المتناهية لا يوجب احتياج الهيولى إلى المتناهي منها، ولا يبعد أن يجاب بأن المقصود من البرهان تحصيل اليقين الدائم، والمطلوب هو موقوف على اليقين الدائم بصحة طريقه، وصحة طرق تصور أطرافه. وقد تقرر في فن البرهان أن العلم ـ أي اليقين الدائم - بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا من جهة الكليات، والحاجة لم يكن إلا إلى صحة الطريق الذي هو للمطلوب، وهو موقوف على العلم بالكلي الذي هو القانون، فثبت الحاجة إلى المنطق في الجملة حاجة حقيقية. ولو تنزلنا سلمنا ما قال المورد لكن لما كان العاصم منحصرا في القانون نسبنا إليه الحاجة، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Thus there must be a law that protects from error in it, i.e., in the arrangement when observed, the definition of the science. And it, i.e., the protecting law, is logic. And they define it by its description as a legal instrument whose observance protects the mind from error in thought. And here there are investigations: The first: that error in particular thoughts does not require anything except whether it is knowledge of a particular path or a universal law; then the need for the more general than logic is established, not for it. The answer: that certain knowledge of particulars is only obtained from universals, so knowledge of particular paths is impossible except from the universals which are the law; thus the need for it is established. And because knowledge of particular paths individually is impossible due to their infinity, there must be a law that is not complete; because the impossibility of some specific aspects of what is needed does not necessitate the impossibility of some possible aspects. Do you not see that the impossibility of infinite corporeal form does not necessitate the matter's need for the finite from it? And it is not far that the answer is that the purpose of demonstration is to obtain perpetual certainty, and what is sought depends on perpetual certainty about the validity of its path and the validity of the paths of conceiving its terms. And it has been established in the art of demonstration that knowledge—i.e., perpetual certainty—of particulars is only obtained from the perspective of universals, and the need is only for the validity of the path that is for the sought thing, and it depends on knowledge of the universal which is the law. Thus the need for logic in general is established as a real need. And if we concede, we accept what the objector said, but since the protector is limited to the law, we attribute the need to it. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines logic by its purpose (bi al-rasm) and defends the necessity of a universal law (qānūn ʻāṣim) to prevent error in thought. The author begins by stating that a protecting law is necessary for correct arrangement (tartīb). This law is logic, described as a legal instrument (ālah qānūniyyah) which, when observed, safeguards the mind from error in reasoning (fī al-fikr). Then the first investigation is raised: if errors occur in particular thoughts, why do we need a universal law like logic? Why not just particular methods? The answer is that certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī) of particulars depends on universals (al-kulliyyāt). Without knowledge of universal principles, particular paths cannot be known. Moreover, the multiplicity of particular cases is infinite, so a universal law is indispensable. The author further illustrates with an analogy: just as the impossibility of an infinite corporeal form does not mean matter has no need for finite forms, so the impossibility of knowing all particulars individually does not negate the need for a universal law. In demonstration (al-burhān), the goal is perpetual certainty (al-yaqīn al-dā’im), which relies on universal principles. Thus, the need for logic is real and justified. The passage concludes by acknowledging a concession, but maintains that since the protector is the law, we attribute the need to it.

الْمَنْطِقُ (al-manṭiq) - logic.
آلة قانونية (ālah qānūniyyah) - a legal instrument.
اليقين الدائم (al-yaqīn al-dā’im) - perpetual certainty.
