Why Propositions Cannot Define a Science
Author argues propositions cannot define science because they are not predicated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the propositions of a science cannot serve as its definition, according to Bahr al-Ulum's commentary.

Core Idea
Propositions are parts, but not predicated, so they cannot define the whole.

In this passage, the author addresses a fundamental question: what defines a science? The propositions (masa'il) are clearly parts of the science, but can they serve as its definition? The answer involves a subtle distinction about predication and the nature of scientific definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? The science itself (e.g., logic, fiqh).
2
Extract the parts
The author says the propositions are 'ajza' ghayr mahmula' (parts not predicated). Find that in the Arabic.
3
Understand why not predicated
The parts are the content, but they are not attributes that can be predicated of the science as a whole. Each proposition is a part, but the science is a collection, not a substance with properties.
4
See the consequence
If the parts are not predicated, they cannot form a definition (hadd) which requires a genus and differentia that are predicated. So defining by listing propositions fails.
5
Contrast with intellectual parts
The author considers the possibility of intellectual parts, but refutes it by noting sciences have imaginary essences, not real essences that allow such parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الشروع شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم حقيقة العلم، مسائله وهي أجزاء غير محمولة، فلا يحد بها، ولأن حده ليست حدا موقوف على معرفة جميع تلك المسائل، فلو كان مقدمة لزم توقف الشروع في تلك المسائل على العلم بها، وهو دور، ولأنه يلزم أن يكون المسائل خارجة عن العلم؛ لأن المقدمة خارجة عن ذلك العلم. وفيه نظر ـ لا لما قيل: إنه يجوز أن يكون للعلم أجزاء عقلية يحد بها، ويتوقف عليه الشروع في المسائل، ويكون إدراكاتها خارجة عن العلم، فلا فساد؛ لأن الأجزاء العقلية والخارجية إنما يكونان معا لماهيات حقيقية، والعلوم لتركبها من مقولات مختلفة ماهيتها اعتبارية، فأين يكون لها أجزاء متحدة في الوجود، وإلا لزم اتحاد المقولات، بل ـ لأن العلم يتعلق بالمسائل بنحوين: علم تصوري وعلم تصديقي، فالعلم بالمسائل بالنحو الأول، بل المسائل المعلومة بهذا النحو من العلم يكون حدا موقوفا عليه، والعلم بالنحو الثاني أو المعلوم بهذا النحو من العلم موقوفا، وخروج المقدمة ليس إلا من العلم التصديقي المتعلق بها، أو المعلوم به، والحد كما يكون من الأجزاء الذهنية كذا يكون من الأجزاء الخارجية. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Introduction to beginning: The commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. The reality of knowledge: its propositions are parts that are not predicated, so it is not defined by them, and because its definition is a definition dependent on knowledge of all those propositions. If it were a premise, then beginning those propositions would depend on knowledge of it, which is circular; and because it would necessitate that the propositions be outside the science, since the premise is outside that science. And there is consideration thereof — not for what is said: it is permissible for knowledge to have intellectual parts by which it is defined, and beginning the propositions depends on it, and their perceptions are outside the science, so there is no corruption; because intellectual and external parts only exist together for real essences, and the sciences, due to their composition from different categories, have an imaginary essence, so where would they have parts united in existence? Otherwise, the categories would be unified. Rather — because knowledge relates to propositions in two ways: conceptual knowledge and assenting knowledge. So knowledge of propositions in the first way, rather the propositions known in this way of knowledge, are a definition dependent on it; and knowledge in the second way, or what is known in this way of knowledge, is dependent. And the exit of the premise is only from the assenting knowledge related to it, or what is known by it. And the definition, just as it is from mental parts, likewise from external parts. This, and Allah knows best the correct.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion that addresses the definition of a science (ilm) and the role of its propositions (masa'il). The author begins by stating the problem: the reality of a science is defined by its propositions, which are 'parts not predicated' (ajza' ghayr mahmula). If the definition of the science itself were a premise (muqaddima) for beginning the study of its propositions, then one would need to know the definition before knowing the propositions, leading to circularity (dawr). Furthermore, the premise would have to be outside the science. The author then considers an objection: someone might say that the science could have intellectual parts (ajza' 'aqliyya) that define it, and those parts are perceived outside the science, so no circularity. The author refutes this by arguing that intellectual and external parts coexist only for real essences (māhiyāt ḥaqīqiyya), but sciences are composed of different categories (maqulat) and have an 'imaginary' essence (i'tibariyya). Thus they cannot have parts that are united in existence. The real resolution lies in the distinction between conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) and assenting knowledge (tasdiq). The propositions known conceptually serve as the definition (hadd) that is prior (mawquf 'alayh), while the premises are outside only from the assenting knowledge (tasdiq). The definition can be composed of both mental and external parts. The key is that the beginning student needs a conceptual grasp, not a demonstrative one.

Key Terms
علم
مقدمة
دور
أجزاء غير محمولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are too many
B) They are not predicated of the science
C) They are unknown to the beginner
D) They are outside the science

Key Takeaway
Propositions are parts of a science but are not predicated of it, so they cannot define it.

Resolving Circularity with Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author resolves circularity by distinguishing conceptual and assenting knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tasawwur/tasdiq distinction to refute circularity in defining a science.

Core Idea
Conceptual knowledge of propositions defines a science; assenting knowledge is the premise outside it.

The author has shown that defining a science by its propositions leads to circularity if the definition itself is a premise. He now offers a sophisticated resolution: knowledge of the propositions comes in two modes. The conceptual grasp (tasawwur) of the propositions serves as the real definition, while the assenting knowledge (tasdiq) is what is outside the science. This dissolves the circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity objection
The author says: if the definition is a premise (muqaddima), then to know the propositions one must know the definition, and to know the definition one must know the propositions -> dawr.
2
Find the author's resolution
He distinguishes two types of knowledge: tasawwur and tasdiq.
3
Apply the distinction
The conceptual knowledge of the propositions (tasawwur) is prior and serves as the definition (hadd) that one needs to begin the study. The assenting knowledge (tasdiq) is what comes later and is outside the science.
4
Understand the role of the definition
The definition is composed of mental parts (concepts) that are known before the detailed propositions.
5
Evaluate the solution
Circularity is avoided because the definition (conceptual knowledge) is not the same as the premise (assenting knowledge).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the author, how does conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the propositions avoid the circularity?
A) It is identical to assenting knowledge
B) It serves as the definition that is prior, not a premise
C) It is impossible
D) It is acquired after study

Key Takeaway
The definition of a science is conceptual knowledge of its propositions, not an assented premise, thus circularity is avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In studying usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'dalil' (evidence) is conceptually known before assenting to its criteria. Explain how this avoids circularity using the tasawwur/tasdiq distinction.

Definition of Logic: External and Inner Speech
Author defines logic as external speech and inner apprehension of intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of logic as defined by the author and explain how logic strengthens both external speech and inner reasoning.

Core Idea
Logic is the art that perfects both our expression and our reasoning.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of logic from our text. The author defines logic concisely as 'speech' and in relation to 'inner speech,' which is the apprehension of intelligibles. Understanding this dual aspect is crucial because it reminds us that logic is not just about how we argue outwardly but also how we think inwardly. Let's break it down.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of 'speech'
The author refers to 'al-takallum' (external speech) and 'al-nutq al-batini' (inner speech). The first is verbal expression; the second is the internal faculty of reason. Notice that both are called 'speech' in Arabic, highlighting the connection between thought and language.
This mirrors the Greek conception of logos.
2
Define inner speech
Inner speech is defined as 'idrak al-ma'qulat' — the apprehension of intelligibles. This means conceptual understanding of universals, i.e., grasping meanings beyond particular instances. This is the core of rational thought.
In logic, this faculty is what we train through studying formal reasoning.
3
Describe the benefit of logic
The art of logic strengthens external speech and leads inner speech to the path of rectitude. So logic improves our ability to express ourselves clearly and to reason correctly.
This is the practical purpose of logic.

It is speech (al-takallum) and concerning inner speech (al-nutq al-batini), which is the apprehension of intelligibles, and this art strengthens the first and leads the second to the path of rectitude.

Commentary
This concise statement defines the art of logic in terms of two faculties: external speech (al-takallum) and inner speech (al-nutq al-batini). Inner speech is the apprehension of intelligibles (idrak al-ma'qulat), meaning conceptual understanding. Logic's benefit is twofold: it strengthens external speech (expression) and guides inner speech (reasoning) to the correct path (sikak al-sidad). This is a classic definition emphasizing logic as a tool for both thought and expression.

The Subject of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles Defined and Critiqued
Author critiques the common definition of secondary intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the standard definition of secondary intelligible and explain the author's objection to it.

Core Idea
Secondary intelligibles are mental, but existence is real—so the common definition is flawed.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental question: what exactly is the subject matter of logic? He begins with the common definition—secondary intelligibles are attributes whose locus is the mind alone—then immediately shows its problem: if existence were merely mental, things would cease to exist when minds vanish. This critique sets the stage for a more refined understanding. Follow the guided steps to unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
The author states: 'the well-known definition of the secondary intelligible is that whose locus of occurrence is the mind only'. Find this phrase in the Arabic.
2
Note the examples
The author gives 'existence and possibility' as examples. These are attributes that seem to be real, not just mental.
3
Understand the objection
If existence were only mental, then when no minds exist, nothing would exist. That is clearly false. The author calls this 'manifestly false for one who refers to intuition'.
4
Identify the author's positive claim
The author asserts that these attributes are real ('awarid fi nafs al-amr). They are not merely mental.
5
Prepare for the resolution
The author then quotes a scholar to distinguish two types of secondary intelligibles. This resolves the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ. وَمَوْضُوعُهُ الْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةُ، المشهور في تعريف المعقول الثاني ما يكون ظرف عروضه الذهن فقط، كالوجود والإمكان ونظائرهما، وحكم بأن القضية المنعقدة منها ذهنية، ولا يخفى أن ذلك صريح البطلان عند من يراجع إلى الوجدان؛ إذ يلزم أن تكذب تلك القضايا عند ارتفاع الأذهان، فيخرج الموجودات عن الوجود، والممكنات عن الإمكان. وأيضا لا معنى لعروض الانتزاعيات إلا كون مَنَاشِيها بحيث يصح الانتزاع عنها، والماهيات الإمكانية والموجودة في نفس الأمر مصححة لانتزاع الإمكان والوجود، والحق أنها عوارض في نفس الأمر. قال صاحب «الأفق المبين»: المعقول الثاني تارة يطلق على ما يعرض الشيء في الذهن كالكلية والجزئية، وهو موضوع المنطق، وتارة على ما يعرض الشيء من غير أن يحاذيه أمر في الخارج، ولا يكون سلبا، ولا أن يكون ...
The subject matter of the science of logic. And its subject matter is the secondary intelligibles. The well-known definition of the secondary intelligible is that whose locus of occurrence is the mind only, like existence and possibility and their analogues. And it has been ruled that the proposition composed from them is mental, and it is not hidden that this is manifestly false for one who refers to intuition; for it would require that those propositions become false when minds cease, so that existents would exit from existence and possibles from possibility. Also there is no meaning to the occurrence of abstracted concepts except that their origins are such that abstraction from them is valid, and the possible and existing essences in actuality are correctifiers for the abstraction of possibility and existence. The truth is that they are attributes in actuality. The author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' said: The secondary intelligible is sometimes applied to what occurs to a thing in the mind, like universality and particularity, and this is the subject of logic; and sometimes to what occurs to a thing without there being something parallel to it in the external, and it is not a negation, nor is it ...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a definition and discussion of the subject matter of logic, which is the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). The author first gives the common definition: a secondary intelligible is an attribute whose locus (zarf) is the mind alone, e.g., existence and possibility. Then he critiques this definition, arguing that if it were true, then propositions containing these concepts would be false when minds cease to exist, which is absurd—existence and possibility are real attributes of things even if no mind conceives them. The author then argues that abstracted concepts (al-intiza'iyyat) are based on real foundations; e.g., possibility is abstracted from possible essences that exist in reality. He concludes that these attributes are real ('awarid fi nafs al-amr). He then quotes al-Ufuq al-Mubin, who distinguishes two types of secondary intelligibles: (1) those that occur to a thing in the mind only, like universality and particularity (this is the subject of logic); (2) those that occur to a thing without a parallel in external reality, and are not negations nor... (the text cuts off). This clarification resolves the confusion: the secondary intelligibles of logic are mental attributes like universality, not existential accidents.

الانتزاعيات

Key Takeaway
The common definition of secondary intelligible is too broad; it must be restricted to purely mental attributes like universality.

Two Types of Secondary Intelligibles: The Subject of Logic Clarified
Author quotes al-Ufuq al-Mubin to clarify two senses of secondary intelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of secondary intelligibles and identify which is the proper subject of logic.

Core Idea
Logic studies only mental attributes like universality, not real attributes like existence.

Having critiqued the broad definition, the author now offers a clarification through the words of al-Ufuq al-Mubin. The term 'secondary intelligible' is ambiguous: it can refer to purely mental attributes (like universality) or to attributes that have no external counterpart (like existence). Only the first is the subject of logic. This distinction saves the definition while preserving the reality of existential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions quoted
Al-Ufuq al-Mubin says: 'the secondary intelligible is sometimes applied to what occurs to a thing in the mind, like universality and particularity'. And 'sometimes to what occurs to a thing without there being something parallel to it in the external'.
2
Understand the first type
This type is purely mental. Universality and particularity do not exist outside the mind; they are attributes we apply to concepts when we think about them.
3
Understand the second type
The second type is something that occurs to a thing but has no external counterpart. Note: the author previously argued that existence does have an external counterpart, so existence might not be in this type. The text cuts off but the reader can infer.
4
Recognize the subject of logic
The author states that the first type is the subject of logic. So logic deals with mental attributes, not with real ones like existence or possibility.
5
Resolve the earlier problem
The common definition only applies to the first type. Existence and possibility are not secondary intelligibles in the logical sense; they are real.

Critique of the Mental Inherence of Second Intelligibles
Critique of the mental-only definition of second intelligibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument against defining second intelligibles as solely mind-inherent and identify the correct account of their inherence.

Core Idea
If second intelligibles inhered only in minds, then all existence would vanish when minds cease—an absurdity.

The standard definition claims second intelligibles inhere only in the mind. The author shows this leads to a contradiction: if propositions about existence were purely mental, they would become false when minds cease, implying existing things cease to exist. This lesson unpacks his reductio and clarifies the true nature of abstract concepts like existence and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Locate the phrase 'ما يكون ظرف عروضه الذهن فقط' and note the examples: existence and possibility. This is the definition the author will critique.
The definition is stated immediately after stating that the subject of logic is the second intelligibles.
2
Outline the reductio argument
The author says: if the definition were true, then when minds cease, the propositions become false, which would make existing things cease to exist and possible things cease to be possible. This is absurd, so the definition is false.
Trace the logical steps: P (definition true) → Q (propositions mental) → R (propositions false when minds cease) → S (existents vanish). S is false, so ¬P.
3
Examine the alternative account
The author says abstract concepts (انتزاعيات) are not merely mental; their sources in reality make abstraction valid. Possible quiddities and real existents are the correctifiers for abstracting possibility and existence.
Focus on the phrase 'الماهيات الإمكانية والموجودة في نفس الأمر مصححة لانتزاع الإمكان والوجود'.
4
Assess the truth of the matter
The author concludes: 'والحق أنها عوارض في نفس الأمر' – these are real accidents, not merely mental. Understand why the mental-only definition fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ ) ) (وَمَوْضُوعُهُ (۲) الْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةِ (۳) ) ، المشهور في تعريف المعقول الثاني ما يكون ظرف عروضه الذهن فقط، كالوجود والإمكان ونظائرهما، وحكم بأن القضية المنعقدة منها ذهنية، ولا يخفى أن ذلك صريح البطلان عند من يراجع إلى الوجدان ؛ إذ يلزم أن تكذب تلك القضايا عند ارتفاع فيخرج الموجودات عن الوجود، والممكنات عن الإمكان. الأذهان، وأيضا لا معنى لعروض الانتزاعيات إلا كون مَنَاشِيها بحيث يصح الانتزاع عنها ، والماهيات الإمكانية والموجودة في نفس الأمر مصححة لانتزاع الإمكان والوجود، والحق أنها عوارض في نفس الأمر . قال صاحب «الأفق المبين»: المعقول الثاني تارة يطلق على ما يعرض الشيء في الذهن كالكلية والجزئية، وهو موضوع المنطق، وتارة على ما يعرض الشيء من غير أن يحاذيه أمر في الخارج، ولا يكون سلبا، ولا أن ينتزع بالإضافة إلى أمر آخر، ولا أن يكون الماهيات مقتضية له، والإمكان ونظائره منه، وهو المستعمل في الفلسفة، وهذا كلام حق، وعبارات الشيخ المقتول تومئ إليه . ولما كان للمعقول الثاني أحوال يبحث عنها في الفلسفة قيده بقوله: (مِنْ حَيْثُ الْإِيصَالُ إِلى مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرٍ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِ (۱))، هذا بظاهره يدل على أن المكتسب بالذات العلم، ويحتمل أن يراد بالتصور المتصور، وبالتصديق المصدق به، هذا كله على رأي القدماء. وأما المتأخرون فقالوا: موضوع المنطق المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي، من حيث الإيصال ؛ بناء على أنه ربما يقع المعقول الثاني محمولا لمسائل هذا الفن فلا يكون موضوعا. ولم يعلموا أن تلك المعقولات أحوال لمعقولات أخرى ، فيجوز وقوعها محمولات من هذه الجهة، كما أنه يجوز وقوعها موضوعات من جهة كونها معقولات ثانية . ثم رد بأن المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي ليس البحث عن أنفسهما؛ إذ ليسا موصلين بهذا الاعتبار، بل بعد كونهما معروضين للمعقولات الثانية ، فتكون هي بالذات موصلة ومبحوثا عنها وفيه كلام؛ لأن الموصل بالذات، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، هو المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، ألا ترى أن الموصل إلى الإنسان الحيوان الناطقُ ، وأما كونه حدا وأمثاله وسائط في أو
The subject matter of logic: and its subject matter is the second intelligibles. The famous definition of the second intelligible is that its locus of inherence is only the mind, like existence, possibility, and their likes, and they ruled that the proposition formed from them is mental. It is not hidden that this is clear falsehood for one who consults his intuition; for it would necessitate that those propositions become false when minds cease, so existing things would exit from existence, and possible things from possibility. Also, there is no meaning to the inherence of abstracted concepts except that their sources are such that abstraction from them is valid, and the possible quiddities and those existing in reality are correctifiers for the abstraction of possibility and existence, and the truth is that they are accidents in reality. The author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' said: the second intelligible is sometimes applied to what inheres in a thing in the mind, like universality and particularity, and that is the subject of logic; and sometimes to what inheres in a thing without there being an external counterpart, and not being a negation, nor being abstracted by relation to another thing, nor being required by the quiddities, and possibility and its likes are from this [type], and it is used in philosophy. And this is true speech, and the expressions of the murdered Shaykh allude to it. Since the second intelligible has states investigated in philosophy, he restricted it with his saying: 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, whether conceptual or assented-to.' This in its apparent meaning indicates that what is acquired primarily is knowledge, and it is possible that by 'conceptual' is meant the conceptualized thing, and by 'assented-to' is meant the thing assented to. All this according to the opinion of the ancients. As for the later scholars, they said: the subject matter of logic is the conceptual and assented-to cognition, from the aspect of leading [to the unknown]; based on the fact that the second intelligible sometimes occurs as a predicate of the propositions of this art, so it would not be a subject. And they did not know that those intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so it is permissible for them to occur as predicates from this aspect, just as it is permissible for them to occur as subjects by virtue of being second intelligibles. Then it was refuted that the conceptual and assented-to cognition is not inquiry about themselves, for they are not leading in this consideration; rather, after they become loci of inherence for the second intelligibles, these [second intelligibles] are the ones that lead primarily and are investigated. There is discussion in this; because what leads primarily, i.e., without intermediary in inherence, is the conceptual and assented-to information. Do you not see that what leads to 'human' is 'rational animal'? As for its being a definition and the like, they are intermediaries in or
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the subject matter (موضوع) of logic. The author begins by stating the standard definition: the subject of logic is the second intelligibles (المعقولات الثانية). He then critiques the common definition of the second intelligible as 'that whose locus of inherence is only the mind' by giving an example: existence and possibility. He argues that if this definition were true, then when minds cease, those propositions would become false, which would mean that existing things would cease to exist and possible things would cease to be possible—an absurd consequence. He further argues that the real criterion for abstracted concepts (انتزاعيات) is the validity of abstraction from their sources, and that possible quiddities and real existents are correctifiers for the abstraction of possibility and existence. Therefore, the truth is that these are real accidents (عوارض في نفس الأمر). Next, the author cites the scholar known as the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' (Mir Muhammad Baqir Damad), who distinguishes two senses of the second intelligible: (1) what inheres in the mind, like universality and particularity—this is the subject of logic; (2) what inheres in a thing without an external counterpart, not being a negation, not being abstracted by relation, and not being required by quiddities—this includes possibility and its likes and is used in philosophy. The author endorses this distinction. Then he explains that in the matn (al-Sullam), the subject is restricted by 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, whether conceptual or assented-to.' This apparent meaning indicates that what is primarily acquired is knowledge (العلم). The author notes that according to the ancients (القدماء), the subject of logic is the second intelligibles. But the later scholars (المتأخرون) argued that the subject is conceptual and assented-to cognition (المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي) because the second intelligible sometimes occurs as a predicate in the propositions of logic, so it would not be a subject. The author criticizes this later view: they did not realize that second intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so they can be both subjects (as second intelligibles) and predicates (as states). Then a further refutation is mentioned: conceptual and assented-to cognitions themselves are not investigated in logic; rather, they become carriers (معروضات) for the second intelligibles, which are the actual objects of investigation. The author adds a comment: what leads primarily is the conceptual and assented-to information itself, because for example, what leads to the concept 'human' is 'rational animal'—the definition. The definition and the like are intermediaries. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is essentially about the correct identification of the subject of logic and whether it is the second intelligibles or the cognitions themselves.

Key Terms
المعقولات الثانية
الانتزاعيات
الذهن
عروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to critique the definition of the second intelligible as solely mind-inherent?
A) Universality and particularity
B) Existence and possibility
C) Humanity and animality
D) Necessity and contingency

Key Takeaway
The second intelligible cannot be defined as purely mind-inherent; its inherence has real sources in external quiddities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept of 'wujub' (obligation) in usul al-fiqh. If one said 'wujub' is purely mental, what reductio follows? Write a one-paragraph refutation using the author's method.

The Two Senses of the Second Intelligible: Logical vs. Philosophical
Explaining Damad's two-sense distinction of second intelligibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical sense of second intelligibles (inhering in the mind) and the philosophical sense (no external counterpart) and apply this distinction to key examples.

Core Idea
The second intelligible has two meanings: one for logic (universals) and one for philosophy (modal concepts like possibility).

The author quotes Damad's 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' to clarify that the term 'second intelligible' is polysemous. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between logic and metaphysics, as each science uses the term in a different sense.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two meanings
Find the sentence: 'المعقول الثاني تارة يطلق على ما يعرض الشيء في الذهن... وتارة على ما يعرض الشيء من غير أن يحاذيه أمر في الخارج...'
2
Understand the first meaning
This is what inheres in the mind, like universality and particularity. This is the subject of logic.
Al-Damad says this is what logic studies.
3
Understand the second meaning
This inheres without an external counterpart, not being a negation, not abstracted by relation, and not required by quiddities. Examples: possibility, existence (according to Damad). This is used in philosophy.
There are four negative conditions: no external counterpart, not negation, not relational, not quidditative.
4
See the author's endorsement
The author says 'وهذا كلام حق، وعبارات الشيخ المقتول تومئ إليه' – he endorses Damad's distinction and links it to Suhrawardi.
The 'murdered Shaykh' is Suhrawardi, who also made similar distinctions.

Source Text

مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ ) ) (وَمَوْضُوعُهُ (۲) الْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةِ (۳) ) ، المشهور في تعريف المعقول الثاني ما يكون ظرف عروضه الذهن فقط، كالوجود والإمكان ونظائرهما، وحكم بأن القضية المنعقدة منها ذهنية، ولا يخفى أن ذلك صريح البطلان عند من يراجع إلى الوجدان ؛ إذ يلزم أن تكذب تلك القضايا عند ارتفاع فيخرج الموجودات عن الوجود، والممكنات عن الإمكان. الأذهان، وأيضا لا معنى لعروض الانتزاعيات إلا كون مَنَاشِيها بحيث يصح الانتزاع عنها ، والماهيات الإمكانية والموجودة في نفس الأمر مصححة لانتزاع الإمكان والوجود، والحق أنها عوارض في نفس الأمر . قال صاحب «الأفق المبين»: المعقول الثاني تارة يطلق على ما يعرض الشيء في الذهن كالكلية والجزئية، وهو موضوع المنطق، وتارة على ما يعرض الشيء من غير أن يحاذيه أمر في الخارج، ولا يكون سلبا، ولا أن ينتزع بالإضافة إلى أمر آخر، ولا أن يكون الماهيات مقتضية له، والإمكان ونظائره منه، وهو المستعمل في الفلسفة، وهذا كلام حق، وعبارات الشيخ المقتول تومئ إليه . ولما كان للمعقول الثاني أحوال يبحث عنها في الفلسفة قيده بقوله: (مِنْ حَيْثُ الْإِيصَالُ إِلى مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرٍ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِ (۱))، هذا بظاهره يدل على أن المكتسب بالذات العلم، ويحتمل أن يراد بالتصور المتصور، وبالتصديق المصدق به، هذا كله على رأي القدماء. وأما المتأخرون فقالوا: موضوع المنطق المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي، من حيث الإيصال ؛ بناء على أنه ربما يقع المعقول الثاني محمولا لمسائل هذا الفن فلا يكون موضوعا. ولم يعلموا أن تلك المعقولات أحوال لمعقولات أخرى ، فيجوز وقوعها محمولات من هذه الجهة، كما أنه يجوز وقوعها موضوعات من جهة كونها معقولات ثانية . ثم رد بأن المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي ليس البحث عن أنفسهما؛ إذ ليسا موصلين بهذا الاعتبار، بل بعد كونهما معروضين للمعقولات الثانية ، فتكون هي بالذات موصلة ومبحوثا عنها وفيه كلام؛ لأن الموصل بالذات، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، هو المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، ألا ترى أن الموصل إلى الإنسان الحيوان الناطقُ ، وأما كونه حدا وأمثاله وسائط في أو
The subject matter of logic: and its subject matter is the second intelligibles. The famous definition of the second intelligible is that its locus of inherence is only the mind, like existence, possibility, and their likes, and they ruled that the proposition formed from them is mental. It is not hidden that this is clear falsehood for one who consults his intuition; for it would necessitate that those propositions become false when minds cease, so existing things would exit from existence, and possible things from possibility. Also, there is no meaning to the inherence of abstracted concepts except that their sources are such that abstraction from them is valid, and the possible quiddities and those existing in reality are correctifiers for the abstraction of possibility and existence, and the truth is that they are accidents in reality. The author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' said: the second intelligible is sometimes applied to what inheres in a thing in the mind, like universality and particularity, and that is the subject of logic; and sometimes to what inheres in a thing without there being an external counterpart, and not being a negation, nor being abstracted by relation to another thing, nor being required by the quiddities, and possibility and its likes are from this [type], and it is used in philosophy. And this is true speech, and the expressions of the murdered Shaykh allude to it. Since the second intelligible has states investigated in philosophy, he restricted it with his saying: 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, whether conceptual or assented-to.' This in its apparent meaning indicates that what is acquired primarily is knowledge, and it is possible that by 'conceptual' is meant the conceptualized thing, and by 'assented-to' is meant the thing assented to. All this according to the opinion of the ancients. As for the later scholars, they said: the subject matter of logic is the conceptual and assented-to cognition, from the aspect of leading [to the unknown]; based on the fact that the second intelligible sometimes occurs as a predicate of the propositions of this art, so it would not be a subject. And they did not know that those intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so it is permissible for them to occur as predicates from this aspect, just as it is permissible for them to occur as subjects by virtue of being second intelligibles. Then it was refuted that the conceptual and assented-to cognition is not inquiry about themselves, for they are not leading in this consideration; rather, after they become loci of inherence for the second intelligibles, these [second intelligibles] are the ones that lead primarily and are investigated. There is discussion in this; because what leads primarily, i.e., without intermediary in inherence, is the conceptual and assented-to information. Do you not see that what leads to 'human' is 'rational animal'? As for its being a definition and the like, they are intermediaries in or
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the subject matter (موضوع) of logic. The author begins by stating the standard definition: the subject of logic is the second intelligibles (المعقولات الثانية). He then critiques the common definition of the second intelligible as 'that whose locus of inherence is only the mind' by giving an example: existence and possibility. He argues that if this definition were true, then when minds cease, those propositions would become false, which would mean that existing things would cease to exist and possible things would cease to be possible—an absurd consequence. He further argues that the real criterion for abstracted concepts (انتزاعيات) is the validity of abstraction from their sources, and that possible quiddities and real existents are correctifiers for the abstraction of possibility and existence. Therefore, the truth is that these are real accidents (عوارض في نفس الأمر). Next, the author cites the scholar known as the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' (Mir Muhammad Baqir Damad), who distinguishes two senses of the second intelligible: (1) what inheres in the mind, like universality and particularity—this is the subject of logic; (2) what inheres in a thing without an external counterpart, not being a negation, not being abstracted by relation, and not being required by quiddities—this includes possibility and its likes and is used in philosophy. The author endorses this distinction. Then he explains that in the matn (al-Sullam), the subject is restricted by 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, whether conceptual or assented-to.' This apparent meaning indicates that what is primarily acquired is knowledge (العلم). The author notes that according to the ancients (القدماء), the subject of logic is the second intelligibles. But the later scholars (المتأخرون) argued that the subject is conceptual and assented-to cognition (المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي) because the second intelligible sometimes occurs as a predicate in the propositions of logic, so it would not be a subject. The author criticizes this later view: they did not realize that second intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so they can be both subjects (as second intelligibles) and predicates (as states). Then a further refutation is mentioned: conceptual and assented-to cognitions themselves are not investigated in logic; rather, they become carriers (معروضات) for the second intelligibles, which are the actual objects of investigation. The author adds a comment: what leads primarily is the conceptual and assented-to information itself, because for example, what leads to the concept 'human' is 'rational animal'—the definition. The definition and the like are intermediaries. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is essentially about the correct identification of the subject of logic and whether it is the second intelligibles or the cognitions themselves.

Key Terms
المعقولات الثانية
الكلية
الجزئية
الإمكان
الأفق المبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin', which of the following is an example of the second intelligible in the philosophical sense?
A) Universality
B) Particularity
C) Possibility
D) Humanity

Key Takeaway
The term 'second intelligible' is ambiguous: in logic it refers to mental attributes like universality, in philosophy to real attributes like possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a second intelligible in the logical sense or philosophical sense: (a) particularity, (b) necessity, (c) humanity (as a universal). Explain your reasoning.

The Subject Matter of Logic: Ancient vs. Later Views
Debate on the subject of logic: ancients vs. later scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the ancient view (subject = second intelligibles) with the later view (subject = conceptual and assented cognitions) and evaluate the refutation of the later view.

Core Idea
Later scholars mistakenly thought second intelligibles could not be subjects; the author argues they can be both subjects and predicates.

After establishing the nature of second intelligibles, the author engages with a major dispute: what is the subject matter of logic? The ancients said second intelligibles; the later scholars said conceptual and assented cognitions. The author sides with the ancients and offers a refutation based on the dual role of second intelligibles as states of other concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find 'هذا كله على رأي القدماء' and 'وأما المتأخرون فقالوا: موضوع المنطق المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي'. Note the contrasting claims.
2
Understand the later scholars' reasoning
They argued that second intelligibles sometimes occur as predicates in logical propositions, so they cannot be subjects. Why would that be problematic for being the subject of a science?
In Aristotelian science, the subject must be the primary thing that predicates inhere in, not the predicate itself.
3
Follow the author's refutation
He says second intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so they can be both predicates (as states) and subjects (as second intelligibles). Then he mentions a further refutation: conceptual cognitions are not investigated themselves; they only become carriers for second intelligibles.
Look for 'ولم يعلموا أن تلك المعقولات أحوال لمعقولات أخرى'.
4
Analyze the author's own comment
He says the primary leader (الموصل بالذات) is conceptual and assented information, because what leads to the concept 'human' is 'rational animal' (definition). So definitions are intermediaries. Understand his point about mediation.
This shows the author does not fully reject the later view but refines it: the primary subject is the cognitions, but the second intelligibles are what are investigated.

The Subject Matter of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles or Primary Concepts
Author analyzes the debate on logic's subject matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the disagreement between the earlier and later logicians on the subject matter of logic and evaluate the arguments for each position.

Core Idea
The subject matter of logic determines whether it is a tool or a science.

In Islamic logic, a foundational debate concerns what exactly logic studies. Does it investigate secondary intelligibles (categories like genus and species) or primary conceptual and assented knowledge? This passage presents the views of earlier and later scholars, along with refutations and refinements. By following this lesson, you will understand the key positions and the logical moves used to defend them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main positions
The earlier scholars say the subject is secondary intelligibles from the aspect of leading to unknown knowledge. The later scholars say it is conceptual and assented known things. This distinction is subtle but crucial.
2
Examine the later scholars' objection
They argue that secondary intelligibles sometimes appear as predicates in the science, so they cannot be the subject. The author claims they overlooked that secondary intelligibles are states of other intelligibles.
3
Follow the rebuttal and counter-rebuttal
The rebuttal says known things are only leading when they are substrates for secondary intelligibles. The counter-rebuttal says known things lead essentially, like 'rational animal' leads to 'human'. This shows the complexity.
4
Understand the teacher's refinement
The teacher argues that the subject is the known thing as a substrate, not the secondary intelligible alone. This reconciles the positions.
5
Reconstruct the author's defense of the earlier view
The author suggests that secondary intelligibles are not mere intermediaries but restrictions on the known thing, so the investigated subject is the restricted compound (known thing + secondary intelligible). Thus the subject is secondary intelligibles as instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع علم المنطق ينتزع بالإضافة إلى أمر آخر، ولا أن يكون الماهيات مقتضية له، والإمكان ونظائره منه، وهو المستعمل في الفلسفة، وهذا كلام حق، وعبارات الشيخ المقتول تومئ إليه . ولما كان للمعقول الثاني أحوال يبحث عنها في الفلسفة قيده بقوله: من حيث الإيصال إلى مجهول تصور أو تصديق، هذا بظاهره يدل على أن المكتسب بالذات العلم، ويحتمل أن يراد بالتصور المتصور، وبالتصديق المصدق به، هذا كله على رأي القدماء. وأما المتأخرون فقالوا: موضوع المنطق المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي، من حيث الإيصال ؛ بناء على أنه ربما يقع المعقول الثاني محمولا لمسائل هذا الفن فلا يكون موضوعا. ولم يعلموا أن تلك المعقولات أحوال لمعقولات أخرى ، فيجوز وقوعها محمولات من هذه الجهة، كما أنه يجوز وقوعها موضوعات من جهة كونها معقولات ثانية . ثم رد بأن المعلوم التصوري والتصديقي ليس البحث عن أنفسهما؛ إذ ليسا موصلين بهذا الاعتبار، بل بعد كونهما معروضين للمعقولات الثانية ، فتكون هي بالذات موصلة ومبحوثا عنها وفيه كلام؛ لأن الموصل بالذات، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، هو المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، ألا ترى أن الموصل إلى الإنسان الحيوان الناطق ، وأما كونه حدا وأمثاله وسائط في الثبوت، فبالحري أن يكون الموضوع هو المعلوم ، كذا قال ذو المناقب العالية ، وصاحب العلوم الفائقة أبونا وأستاذنا قدس سره . وحمل عبارة الكتاب عليه ممكن أيضا، لكن لا يرخصه الحاشية المنقولة عنه رحمه الله تعالى . ولك أن تنصر القدماء بأنه هب أن الموصل المعلوم، لكن لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنه معروض لها ، وليست المعقولات الثانية وسائط في الثبوت فقط ، بل قيودا للموصل، فالمبحوث عنه هذا المقيد، وهو مرادهم بكون الموضوع المعقولات الثانية ؛ إذ ليس المراد معانيها ، بل مصاديقها من حيث إنها مصاديقها ، فتأمل فيه . وذهب المتأخرون إلى أن موضوعه المعقولات التصورية
The subject matter of logic derives by way of addition to another thing, and not that the essences necessitate it, and possibility and its likes are from it, and it is what is used in philosophy, and this is true speech, and the expressions of the murdered Shaykh allude to it. And since the secondary intelligible has states investigated in philosophy, he restricted it by his saying: 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, conceptual or assented.' This, by its apparent meaning, indicates that what is acquired essentially is knowledge, and it is possible that by 'conceptual' is meant the concept, and by 'assented' is meant the object of assent. All this is according to the opinion of the earlier scholars. As for the later scholars, they said: The subject matter of logic is the conceptual and assented known things, from the aspect of leading; based on the fact that sometimes the secondary intelligible occurs as predicate of the problems of this art and so is not a subject. And they did not know that those intelligibles are states of other intelligibles, so it is permissible for them to occur as predicates from this aspect, just as it is permissible for them to occur as subjects from the aspect of being secondary intelligibles. Then it was refuted that the conceptual and assented known things are not investigated in themselves, for they are not leading in this consideration, but rather after they become substrates for secondary intelligibles, so they are essentially leading and investigated, and in this there is discussion; because what leads essentially, i.e., without intermediary in attribution, is the conceptual and assented information. Do you not see that what leads to 'human' is 'rational animal'? And as for its being a definition and its likes, they are intermediaries in confirmation, so it is more appropriate that the subject is the known thing. So said the possessor of lofty merits, the master of excellent sciences, our father and teacher, may his secret be sanctified. And applying the wording of the book to it is also possible, but the transmitted gloss from him, may God have mercy on him, does not permit it. And you can support the earlier scholars by saying: Granted that what leads is the known thing, but not absolutely, rather from the aspect that it is a substrate for them, and the secondary intelligibles are not intermediaries in confirmation only, but rather restrictions on what leads, so what is investigated is this restricted thing, and that is their intention in saying that the subject is the secondary intelligibles; for what is meant is not their meanings, but rather their instances from the aspect that they are their instances. So reflect on it. And the later scholars held that its subject is the conceptual intelligibles.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the subject matter of logic, a central dispute in the tradition. The author first presents the position of the earlier scholars (qudama): the subject of logic is the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniyya) from the aspect of leading to the unknown. He explains that this is indicated by the definition in the matn: 'from the aspect of leading to an unknown, conceptual or assented.' Then he presents the view of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun), who argue that the subject is the conceptual and assented known things (al-ma'lumat al-tasawwuriyya wa al-tasdiqiyya) because sometimes a secondary intelligible appears as a predicate in the art and thus cannot be the subject. The author criticizes this, saying they overlooked that secondary intelligibles are states of other intelligibles and can be either predicate or subject depending on perspective. He then reports a rebuttal: conceptual and assented known things are not investigated in themselves; they only become leading when they are substrates for secondary intelligibles, so the latter are essentially the objects of investigation. However, he notes a counterargument: what leads essentially is the known thing, not the secondary intelligible, as the definition 'rational animal' leads to 'human' directly. The author then quotes his teacher's view supporting the position that the subject is the known thing as a substrate. Finally, he offers a way to defend the earlier scholars: the secondary intelligibles are not just intermediaries but restrictions on the known thing, and what is investigated is the restricted compound. Thus, the subject is the secondary intelligibles understood as instances (masadiq) of those concepts, not their abstract meanings. The passage ends by noting the later scholars' alternative view that the subject is conceptual intelligibles.

Key Terms
موضوع علم المنطق
المعقولات الثانية
المعقولات التصورية والتصديقية

Key Takeaway
The debate on the subject matter of logic hinges on whether logic studies secondary intelligibles directly or the primary concepts that are their substrates, with the preferred view being that the subject is the secondary intelligibles as instances.

The Subject of Logic: A Foundational Debate
Author discusses the debate on the subject matter of logic between earlier and later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the earlier and later scholars' views on the subject of logic and identify the role of secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
Logic's subject is either the known or secondary intelligibles, marking a deep divide in Islamic epistemology.

The subject matter of a science defines its scope and purpose. In logic, the debate between earlier and later scholars centers on whether logic studies 'the known' absolutely or specifically 'secondary intelligibles'—mental attributes of concepts. This passage from a commentary on Sullum al-Ulum unpacks that debate, adding nuance about how 'the known' may be restricted. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of what logic can and cannot achieve as an instrument of thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main positions
Locate where the author states the earlier view (subject = secondary intelligibles) and the later view (subject = conceptual intelligibles). Note the Arabic terms: 'ma'qulat thaniya' and 'ma'qulat tasawwuriyya'.
2
Parse the nuance about 'the known'
The author suggests a middle path: 'the known' can be the subject if it is restricted by being an attribute of the secondary intelligibles. Understand that this is not a third view but a defense of the earlier position.
Key phrase: 'fal-mabhooth 'anhu hadha al-muqayyad'
3
Trace the commentary layers
Identify the base text (the matn), the commentator (the author's teacher), and the gloss (hāshiya). See how each layer shifts the interpretation: the teacher says the subject is the known; the gloss rejects that; the author reconciles by restricting the known.
4
Contrast the implications for other sciences
Consider how each view would affect the role of logic in usul al-fiqh or kalam. For example, if logic studies only secondary intelligibles, it cannot directly judge external realities; if it studies the known, it may claim broader applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمهات شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم الثبوت، فبالحري أن يكون الموضوع هو المعلوم، كذا قال ذو المناقب العالية، وصاحب العلوم الفائقة أبونا وأستاذنا قدس سره. وحمل عبارة الكتاب عليه ممكن أيضا، لكن لا يرخصه الحاشية المنقولة عنه رحمه الله تعالى. ولك أن تنصر القدماء بأنه هب أن الموصل المعلوم، لكن لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنه معروض لها، وليست المعقولات الثانية وسائط في الثبوت فقط، بل قيودا للموصل، فالمبحوث عنه هذا المقيد، وهو مرادهم بكون الموضوع المعقولات الثانية؛ إذ ليس المراد معانيها، بل مصاديقها من حيث إنها مصاديقها، فتأمل فيه. وذهب المتأخرون إلى أن موضوعه المعقولات التصورية.
Mothers of explanation of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullum al-Ulum: certainty, so it is fitting that the subject is the known. Thus said the possessor of high merits and the owner of superior sciences, our father and teacher, may his secret be sanctified. Interpreting the expression of the book upon it is also possible, but the transmitted gloss from him, may Allah have mercy on him, does not permit it. And you may support the earlier scholars by saying: suppose that the conveyer is the known, but not absolutely, rather insofar as it is an attribute of them [the known], and the secondary intelligibles are not only means in certainty, but also restrictions on the conveyer. So the investigated matter is this restricted thing, and that is their intention in saying the subject is the secondary intelligibles; for the intended is not their meanings, but their instances insofar as they are instances. So contemplate it. And the later scholars held that its subject is the conceptual intelligibles.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the subject matter of logic. The author begins by stating a position that the subject is 'the known' (al-ma'lum), as claimed by his teacher. He then notes that interpreting the text of the book (likely the matn) in this way is possible but is rejected by a transmitted gloss from the same teacher. The author then offers a defense of the earlier scholars (al-qadama): they do not claim that the conveyer (al-muwasil) is the known absolutely, but only insofar as it is an attribute of the known. The secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) are not just means to certainty but also restrictions (quyud) on the conveyer. Thus the subject is the known restricted by being an attribute of these intelligibles. The intended is not the meanings of the secondary intelligibles but their instances (masadiq) insofar as they are instances. Finally, the author reports that the later scholars (al-muta'akhirun) held that the subject is the conceptual intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-tasawwuriyya). This illustrates a key dispute in Islamic logic: whether the subject is the known in general or specifically the secondary intelligibles considered as mental attributes.

Classification of Interrogative Particles in Islamic Logic
Author classifies interrogative particles and discusses priority of quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Arabic interrogative particles into derivatives of 'ayy' or compounds of 'hal' and explain the logical priority of quidditative over existential inquiry.

Core Idea
Every interrogative particle has a specific domain; together they form the logical toolkit for essential and existential questions.

In this lesson, we analyze a passage from the introduction of a logic text that classifies five key interrogative particles: man (who), kam (how many), kayfa (how), ayna (where), and mata (when). The author groups them as either derivatives of the particle 'ayy' (which) or as instances of the compound interrogative 'hal' (whether). He then makes a crucial logical point: the question of essence (ma'iyya haqiqiyya) should precede the question of existence (hal murakkaba) in inquiry, because a thing's existence cannot be understood apart from its quiddity. By mastering this classification, you will understand how Islamic logicians structure fundamental inquiries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five particles
Locate the five interrogative particles listed: man (من), kam (كم), kayfa (كيف), ayna (أين), and mata (متى). Note the specific logical domain assigned to each: identity, quantity, state, place, and time.
These are standard in Arabic grammar and logic.
2
Classify each particle
According to the author, man is a derivative of 'ayy' (أي), while the remaining four are subsumed under the compound 'hal' (الهل المركبة). Trace this grouping in the Arabic: فهي إما ذنابات لـ أي كمن، أو مندرجة في الهل المركبة كالبواقي.
The term ذنابات means 'tails' or 'branches'.
3
Distinguish existential vs. quidditative questions
The compound hal (الهل المركبة) asks about existence (هل هو موجود؟). The true quidditative question (المائية الحقيقية) asks about essence (ما هو؟). Recognize that these two types of inquiry serve different logical purposes.
In later logic, hal can be simple or compound; here 'compound' refers to a full propositional question.
4
Analyze the priority claim
The author states that there is no necessary order between the two questions, but it is preferable (الأولى) to start with the quidditative. This is because existence cannot be conceived without some essence. Reflect on the logical basis: knowing what something is precedes knowing whether it exists.
This mirrors the famous Ashʿari position that essence is prior to existence in the mind.
5
Apply to a real example
Consider defining 'human' in Islamic theology. The quidditative question is 'What is a human?' (answering with genus and differentia: rational animal). The existential question is 'Does a human exist?' The logical order requires answering the quidditative first to give content to the existential.
This principle appears in debates about the proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا مَطْلَبُ مَنْ لِطَلَبِ التَّعَيُّنِ الشَّخْصِيِّ، وَكَمْ لِلْعَدَدِ، وَكَيْفَ لِلسُّؤَالِ عَنِ الْحَالِ، وَأَيْنَ لِلسُّؤَالِ عَنِ الْمَكَانِ، وَمَتَى لِلزَّمَانِ، فَهِيَ إِمَّا ذَنَابَاتٌ، أَيْ تَوَابِعُ لِأَيَّ، كَمَنْ، أَوْ مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي الْهَلِ الْمُرَكَّبَةِ، كَالْبَوَاقِي. وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ. يَعْلَمُ وُجُودَ الشَّيْءِ لَمْ يُمْكِنْ أَنْ يُتَصَوَّرَ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْجُودٌ وَلَا يَكُونُ التَّرْتِيبُ ضَرُورِيًّا بَيْنَ الْهَلِيَّةِ الْمُرَكَّبَةِ وَالْمَائِيَّةِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ، لَكِنَّ الْأَوْلَى تَقْدِيمُ الْمَائِيَّةِ.
As for the inquiry 'who' for asking about personal identity, 'how many' for number, 'how' for asking about condition, 'where' for asking about place, and 'when' for time, they are either tails, i.e., branches of 'which', like 'who', or included in the compound 'whether', like the rest. And God knows best what is correct. The existence of a thing cannot be conceived insofar as it exists, and there is no necessary order between the compound existential proposition and the true quidditative proposition, but the preferred is to present the quidditative.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage, taken from the end of the introduction, first classifies the five interrogative particles: 'man' (who) for specific identity, 'kam' (how many) for number, 'kayfa' (how) for state, 'ayna' (where) for place, and 'mata' (when) for time. These are either derived from the particle 'ayy' (which) — like 'man' — or are subsumed under the compound interrogative 'hal' (whether). The author then notes that the existence of a thing cannot be conceptualized from the aspect of its existence alone, and there is no necessary priority between the compound existential question (hal al-murakkaba) and the true quidditative question (ma'iyya haqiqiyya); however, it is preferable to begin with the quidditative question. This reflects the logical principle that essence (mahiyya) is prior to existence in conceptualization.

The Six Types of Signification
Classification of signification into six types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three grounds of signification (intellectual, conventional, natural) and identify whether a given example is verbal or non-verbal.

Core Idea
Signification divides into three grounds: intellectual, conventional, natural; each has verbal and non-verbal forms.

Communication depends on signification, but not all signification works the same way. Islamic logicians classify it based on how the signifier connects to the signified. This lesson unpacks the threefold classification based on 'illah (ground) and its application to both words and non-linguistic signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three grounds
Find the three categories in the Arabic: عقلية (intellectual), وضعية (conventional), طبيعية (natural). Note the basis for each: essential relation, positing, and natural production.
Each ground is a distinct way the signifier relates to the signified.
2
Understand the definition of each
Intellectual requires a rational relation like causality; conventional requires knowledge of the positing; natural is spontaneous, like a cry of pain.
3
Apply the verbal/non-verbal distinction
Each ground can be expressed through words (لفظية) or non-linguistic signs (غير لفظية). So there are six total types.
4
Test examples
Determine the ground and type for examples: smoke indicating fire (natural non-verbal), the word 'fire' (conventional verbal), cause implying effect (intellectual non-verbal).
Islamic examples only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث في الدلالة وما يتعلق بها. الإفادة إنما تتم بالدلالة، فلا بد من البحث عنها؛ لإفادة زيادة يتعلق بها البصيرة. منها عقلية بعلاقة ذاتية، كالعلية واللزوم، ومنها وضعية بجعل الجاعل، بحيث إذا علم المجعول علم المعنى، بشرط العلم بالجعل، ومنها طبيعية، بإحداث الطبيعة الدال عند عروض المدلول، وكل منها لفظية وغير لفظية، فهذه ستة أقسام. وإذا كان الإنسان مدني الطبع، أي يعسر معيشته إلا مع بني كثير الافتقار إلى التعليم والتعلم، وكانت اللفظية الوضعية أعدها للمعاني وأسهلها؛ لكفايتها الغرض الذي هو العلم بالوضع، وعدم الحاجة إلى التدقيق في حصول الإفادة، فلها الاعتبار في العلوم. ومن هنا تبين أن الألفاظ موضوعة للمعاني، من حيث هي.
A section on signification and what relates to it. Communication is only completed by signification, so it is necessary to investigate it for the benefit of additional insight related to it. Among them is intellectual signification by an essential relation, such as causality and entailment; and among them is conventional signification by the positing of a positor, such that if the posited is known, the meaning is known, with the condition of knowledge of the positing; and among them is natural signification, by nature producing the signifier upon occurrence of the signified. And each of these is verbal and non-verbal. So these are six divisions. And since the human being is social by nature, i.e., his livelihood is difficult except with many [others], greatly in need of teaching and learning, and conventional verbal signification is the most comprehensive for meanings and the easiest, because it suffices for the purpose which is knowledge of the convention, and there is no need for precision in achieving communication, it has consideration in the sciences. And from here it is clear that words are placed for meanings, insofar as they are.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies signification (dalala) as a prelude to discussing words (alfaz). The author, Bahr al-Ulum, explains that communication (ifada) depends on signification, so it must be studied. Signification is divided into three categories based on the ground that connects the signifier and signified: intellectual ('aqliyya) based on an essential relation like causality and entailment; conventional (wad'iyya) based on the deliberate positing by a positor (ja'il) where knowledge of the posited thing leads to knowledge of the meaning provided one knows the positing; and natural (tabi'iyya) where nature itself causes the signifier to appear when the signified occurs, like a groan from pain. Each of these three can be either verbal (lafziyya) or non-verbal (ghayr lafziyya), yielding six total types. The author then argues that humans are inherently social (madani al-tab') and need teaching and learning, and among all types, conventional verbal signification is the most comprehensive (a'ammuha) and easiest (ashalluha) because it directly conveys meanings via agreed-upon words without requiring deeper investigation. Therefore, it is the primary type used in sciences. The passage concludes that words (alfaz) are set for meanings as such.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalala): Signification; the relation between a signifier (dal) and a signified (madlul) that enables understanding of the signified from the signifier. [Note: In earlier logic, dalala often meant formal implication; here it is broadened to include three types.]
عقلية ('aqliyya): Intellectual signification based on an essential rational relation like causation or logical entailment, not requiring convention or natural instinct. [Note: Stable across tradition, but sometimes distinguished from عقلي by Shafi'i usul.]
وضعية (wad'iyya): Conventional signification established by a positor (ja'il) where the meaning is known once the posited sign is known, contingent on awareness of the positing. [Note: Central in logic and usul; consistent use.]
طبيعية (tabi'iyya): Natural signification where nature itself produces the signifier (e.g., a cry of pain) when the signified occurs, without convention or intellectual inference. [Note: Some later logicians like al-Sayyid al-Sharif denied its existence in non-verbal forms; al-Dawwani affirmed it.]
لفظية (lafziyya): Verbal signification where the signifier is a spoken word or utterance. [Note: No significant shift.]
غير لفظية (ghayr lafziyya): Non-verbal signification where the signifier is not a word, e.g., smoke indicating fire. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of signification depends on a 'positor' (ja'il) establishing a connection?
A) Intellectual signification
B) Conventional signification
C) Natural signification
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Islamic logicians classify signification into three grounds—intellectual, conventional, and natural—each having verbal and non-verbal forms.

Why Conventional Verbal Signification is Primary in the Sciences
Argument for primacy of conventional verbal signification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conventional verbal signification is considered the most comprehensive and easiest for human communication, using the author's reasoning about human social nature.

Core Idea
Because humans are social and need efficient teaching, conventional words are the primary tool for scientific communication.

After classifying six types, the text argues that conventional verbal signification (wad'iyya lafziyya) is the most important for sciences. Why? Because humans are social (madani) and require teaching and learning; words can convey meanings directly without needing further inference. This lesson analyses that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Find the sentence: 'wa idha kana al-insan madani al-tab'...' which states humans are social and need teaching and learning.
This premise grounds the argument.
2
Connect to signification
The author then claims that conventional verbal signification is the most comprehensive and easiest because it suffices for conveying meanings with just knowledge of the convention.
3
Note the conclusion
Therefore, it has consideration (al-i'tibar) in the sciences.
4
Reflect on implication
Words are placed for meanings as such (al-alfaz mawdu'a lil-ma'ani min haythu hiya). This is a foundational principle for logic and usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث في الدلالة وما يتعلق بها. الإفادة إنما تتم بالدلالة، فلا بد من البحث عنها؛ لإفادة زيادة يتعلق بها البصيرة. منها عقلية بعلاقة ذاتية، كالعلية واللزوم، ومنها وضعية بجعل الجاعل، بحيث إذا علم المجعول علم المعنى، بشرط العلم بالجعل، ومنها طبيعية، بإحداث الطبيعة الدال عند عروض المدلول، وكل منها لفظية وغير لفظية، فهذه ستة أقسام. وإذا كان الإنسان مدني الطبع، أي يعسر معيشته إلا مع بني كثير الافتقار إلى التعليم والتعلم، وكانت اللفظية الوضعية أعدها للمعاني وأسهلها؛ لكفايتها الغرض الذي هو العلم بالوضع، وعدم الحاجة إلى التدقيق في حصول الإفادة، فلها الاعتبار في العلوم. ومن هنا تبين أن الألفاظ موضوعة للمعاني، من حيث هي.
A section on signification and what relates to it. Communication is only completed by signification, so it is necessary to investigate it for the benefit of additional insight related to it. Among them is intellectual signification by an essential relation, such as causality and entailment; and among them is conventional signification by the positing of a positor, such that if the posited is known, the meaning is known, with the condition of knowledge of the positing; and among them is natural signification, by nature producing the signifier upon occurrence of the signified. And each of these is verbal and non-verbal. So these are six divisions. And since the human being is social by nature, i.e., his livelihood is difficult except with many [others], greatly in need of teaching and learning, and conventional verbal signification is the most comprehensive for meanings and the easiest, because it suffices for the purpose which is knowledge of the convention, and there is no need for precision in achieving communication, it has consideration in the sciences. And from here it is clear that words are placed for meanings, insofar as they are.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies signification (dalala) as a prelude to discussing words (alfaz). The author, Bahr al-Ulum, explains that communication (ifada) depends on signification, so it must be studied. Signification is divided into three categories based on the ground that connects the signifier and signified: intellectual ('aqliyya) based on an essential relation like causality and entailment; conventional (wad'iyya) based on the deliberate positing by a positor (ja'il) where knowledge of the posited thing leads to knowledge of the meaning provided one knows the positing; and natural (tabi'iyya) where nature itself causes the signifier to appear when the signified occurs, like a groan from pain. Each of these three can be either verbal (lafziyya) or non-verbal (ghayr lafziyya), yielding six total types. The author then argues that humans are inherently social (madani al-tab') and need teaching and learning, and among all types, conventional verbal signification is the most comprehensive (a'ammuha) and easiest (ashalluha) because it directly conveys meanings via agreed-upon words without requiring deeper investigation. Therefore, it is the primary type used in sciences. The passage concludes that words (alfaz) are set for meanings as such.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalala): Signification; the relation between a signifier (dal) and a signified (madlul) that enables understanding of the signified from the signifier. [Note: In earlier logic, dalala often meant formal implication; here it is broadened to include three types.]
وضعية لفظية (wad'iyya lafziyya): Conventional verbal signification; the most comprehensive and easiest type according to the author, used as the primary means in sciences. [Note: No significant shift.]
مدني الطبع (madani al-tab'): Social by nature; refers to the human disposition requiring cooperation and teaching. [Note: Classical concept from Islamic political philosophy.]

The Three Types of Verbal Signification
Author defines the three types of verbal signification: mutabaqa, tadhammun, and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which type of verbal signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, or iltizam) a given word-meaning relationship falls into.

Core Idea
A single word can signify its whole meaning, a part, or something necessarily attached—only iltizam requires a validating relationship.

When you hear the word 'ṣalāh', what comes to mind? Its full meaning, a part of it, or something else? Classical logicians classified these possibilities into three categories: mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam. This lesson will equip you to parse any verbal signification according to these categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coined meaning
For a given word, determine the conventional meaning for which it was originally coined (al-wadʿ al-aṣlī). This is the entire meaning that the word directly signifies.
2
Check for mutabaqa
If the word is used to signify that entire coined meaning, it is mutabaqa. Example: the word 'man' signifying the whole animal rational.
Mutabaqa is the default signification.
3
Check for tadammun
If the word signifies only a part of that coined meaning, it is tadammun. Example: 'man' signifying 'animal' (which is part of its definition).
Tadammun requires the part to be inherent in the whole.
4
Check for iltizam
If the word signifies something external to its coined meaning, but necessarily connected to it by a rational or customary relationship (ʿalāqa), it is iltizam. Example: 'roof' signifying 'wall'.
The ʿalāqa must be present; without it, iltizam is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى تَمَامِ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ تَمَامُ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، مُطَابَقَةٌ. وَعَلَى جُزْئِهِ، مِنْ تِلْكَ الْحَيْثِيَّةِ، تَضَمُّنٌ. وَدَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى الْخَارِجِ، مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ خَارِجٌ، الْتِزَامٌ. وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ عَلَاقَةٍ مُصَحِّحَةٍ لِلِانْتِقَالِ مِنْهُ إِلَى الْخَارِجِ، عَقْلِيَّةٍ أَوْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ.
The signification of a word for the entirety of what it was coined for, in terms of it being the entirety of what it was coined for, is 'correspondence' (muṭābaqah). And its signification for a part of it, from that perspective, is 'inclusion' (taḍammun). And its signification for what is external [to the meaning], in terms of it being external, is 'commitment' (iltizām). And there must be a relationship that justifies the transition from it to the external, either rational or customary.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the three standard types of verbal signification (dalāla lafẓiyya wadʿiyya) in Islamic logic. The author first defines muṭābaqah (correspondence): when a word signifies the entire meaning for which it was coined, such as the word 'man' signifying the whole animal rational. Next, taḍammun (inclusion): when the same word signifies a part of that meaning, e.g., 'man' signifying 'animal' (a part of the definition). Finally, iltizām (commitment): when the word signifies something external to its coined meaning, but necessarily associated with it, such as 'roof' signifying 'wall' (by rational connection) or 'generous' signifying 'generosity' (by customary connection). The author emphasises that for iltizām, a valid relationship (ʿalāqa) is required, either rational (luzūm ʿaqlī, e.g., creature implying creator) or customary (ʿurfī, e.g., the word 'lion' signifying bravery). This classification is fundamental for understanding how words convey meaning beyond the immediate lexical definition, especially in legal and theological reasoning where implied meanings (dalīl al-khiṭāb) are often invoked.

Key Terms
مطابقة
تضمن
التزام
علاقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Verbal signification is either for the whole meaning (mutabaqa), a part (tadammun), or an external necessary meaning (iltizam), with the last requiring a valid relationship.
