The Three Types of Verbal Signification
Author defines the three types of verbal signification: matching, inclusion, and implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three types of verbal signification (mutabaqah, tadamun, iltizam) in a given word-meaning relationship and explain the basis of the necessary connection (rational or conventional).

Core Idea
Words signify in three ways: whole meaning, part of meaning, or a necessary associated meaning.

In this passage, the author lays out the fundamental classification of how words convey meaning beyond their literal definitions. Understanding these three types—mutabaqah, tadamun, and iltizam—is essential for any student of the Islamic sciences, because it underpins how scholars reason from texts to rulings, whether in usul al-fiqh, tafsir, or kalam. This lesson will equip you to recognize each type and understand the conditions for implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its conventional meaning
Start with a given Arabic word and determine its literal or metaphorical meaning as established by convention (wad'). For example, 'insan' (man) signifies the whole concept of a rational animal.
Convention includes both haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical).
2
Classify the relation: whole, part, or external
Decide whether the signified meaning is the entire meaning for which the word was coined (mutabaqah), a part of that meaning (tadamun), or something external but necessarily associated (iltizam). For 'insan': the whole meaning is mutabaqah; 'animal' alone is tadamun; 'the ability to write' could be iltizam if there is a necessary association.
Matching includes both literal and metaphorical uses as long as the word signifies the entire intended meaning.
3
Determine the basis of necessity for iltizam
If the signification is iltizam, identify whether the necessary connection is rational (e.g., ceiling implies wall) or conventional (e.g., a word implies its religious connotation). The author notes that there is scholarly consideration regarding the two scales.
The 'two scales' (al-mizaniyyin) likely refer to logicians who differ on this classification.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take a term from fiqh or kalam and analyze its significations. For instance, 'salah' by mutabaqah signifies the entire prayer ritual; by tadamun, it can signify a part like ruku'; by iltizam, it signifies the spiritual benefit or the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالدلالة اللفظية الوضعية فهم شيء من اللفظ بعلاقة الوضع، مجازيا كان أو حقيقيا، وهي على تمام ما استعمل فيه مطابقة، فيدخل فيه الدلالات المجازية، وعلى جزئه المنفهم في ضمن فهم الكل تضمن، وعلى الخارج المنفهم بالتبع التزام، ولا بد من اللزوم عقلا أو عرفا، لكن في النسبة إلى الميزانيين تأمل
The conventional verbal signification is the understanding of something from the word by virtue of a conventional relation, whether metaphorical or literal. And it is [called] 'matching' when [the understood meaning] is the entirety of what the word is used for; this includes metaphorical significations. And it is [called] 'inclusion' when [the understood meaning] is a part of what is understood in the whole understanding. And it is [called] 'implication' when [the understood meaning] is something external understood as a consequence. And the necessary relation must be either rational or conventional. However, regarding the two scales [i.e., the two logicians] there is consideration.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of conventional verbal signification (al-dalalah al-lafziyyah al-wad'iyyah). The author begins by stating that such signification is understanding something from a word based on a conventional relation, whether the word is used literally (haqiqah) or metaphorically (majaz). The first type is 'matching' (mutabaqah), where the word signifies the entirety of what it was coined for. For example, the word 'man' signifies the entire concept of man. The second type is 'inclusion' (tadamun), where the word signifies a part of the whole meaning; e.g., 'man' signifies the animality part of man. The third is 'implication' (iltizam), where the word signifies something external to its original meaning but necessarily connected, such as the word 'ceiling' signifying the wall. The author notes that the necessary connection must be either rational (aqlan) or conventional (urfan). However, he adds a caution: regarding the two scales (al-mizaniyyin, likely referring to logicians who emphasize the two scales of intellect and convention) there is consideration (tammul). This indicates that the classification may differ among scholars.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalalah): Understanding something from a word by virtue of a conventional relation, whether literal or metaphorical. [Note: In earlier logic texts, dalalah often refers broadly to any indication; here it is specifically verbal and conventional.]
مطابقة (mutabaqah): Signification of the entire meaning for which the word was coined, including metaphorical usage.
تضمن (tadamun): Signification of a part of the whole meaning understood from the word.
التزام (iltizam): Signification of something external to the word's meaning but necessarily associated, due to rational or conventional necessity. [Note: Later scholars debated whether iltizam is purely rational or conventional; this passage notes both possibilities.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) مجاز (metaphorical)

Key Takeaway
Verbal signification operates on three levels: whole (mutabaqah), part (tadamun), and necessary association (iltizam); the last requires a rational or conventional link.

Relations Among the Three Types of Indication: Mutabaqa, Tadammun, and Iltizam
Author explains necessary relationships among the three types of linguistic indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary entailment relations among mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and explain why the converse does not hold.

Core Idea
Tadammun and iltizam necessarily entail mutabaqa, but not vice versa.

The three types of linguistic indication are foundational to Islamic logic. This passage clarifies the logical dependencies among them, resolving a key point of debate between the author and the Mīzāniyān. Understanding these relations is crucial for correctly interpreting definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three indications
Define mutabaqa (literal correspondence), tadammun (inclusion of a part), and iltizam (implication of an external meaning). Note that tadammun and iltizam are said to entail mutabaqa.
Mutabaqa is the primary indication; the others depend on it.
2
Analyze the entailment from tadammun/iltizam to mutabaqa
The passage states that both tadammun and iltizam necessarily imply mutabaqa. This means if a word indicates by inclusion or implication, it must also have a literal meaning.
3
Examine the lack of converse
Mutabaqa does not entail tadammun (because of simple meanings like 'existence') nor iltizam (because a word cannot be used literally and metaphorically at the same time, according to the author).
The author resolves this by introducing 'estimative entailment' (luzum taqdiri): a hypothetical scenario where if the word were used for that meaning, it would be mutabaqa.
4
Assess the relationship between tadammun and iltizam
The passage concludes that tadammun and iltizam do not entail each other. Each can occur without the other.
For example, a simple concept has tadammun (if complex) or iltizam but not necessarily both.
5
Compare the author's view with that of the Mīzāniyān
The author (likely the matn writer) holds that iltizam is metaphorical, preventing simultaneous literal use. The Mīzāniyān allow iltizam to be a necessary concomitant rationally or conventionally, making the entailment clearer.
This shows how differing definitions affect logical relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ. الدلالات الثلاث بعضها مع بعض. ويلزمهما المطابقة، أي يلزم التضمن والالتزام المطابقة، وهذا بيان نسب ظاهر على رأي الميزانيين وبعض الأجلة، وغير ظاهر على رأي المصنف؛ إذ الالتزام عنده الدلالة على الخارج المستعمل فيه اللفظ مجازا، ولا يصح استعمال لفظ واحد حقيقة ومجازا معا، فلا يوجد دلالته على ما وضع له، إلا أن يقال: اللزوم تقديري بمعنى أن ههنا معنى لو استعمل فيه اللفظ يكون دلالته عليه مطابقة، ولا عكس، أي ولا يلزمها التضمن؛ لوجود ألفاظ وضعت لمعان بسيطة، والالتزام، وهو مشكل إن أريد اللزوم التقديري على رأي المصنف؛ إذ ما من معنى إلا وله خارج، لو استعمل فيه اللفظ كان دلالته عليه التزاما، وأما على رأي الميزانيين وبعض الأجلة فظاهر؛ لجواز أن يوضع لازم له عقلا وعرفا. لا لفظ بإزاء معنى ولما زعم الإمام أنه ما من معنى إلا وله لازم عقلي، وأقله ليس غيره دفعه بقوله: (وَكَوْنُهُ) لَيْسَ غَيْرَهُ لَيْس ممَّا يسبق إليه الذهن دائما. فهمه فهمه عقلا أو عرفا، وأما التضمنية والالتزامية فلا لزوم بينهما. عدم استلزام الالتزام إياه ظاهر؛ لتحققه في البسائط أيضا، وأما عدم استلزام التضمن إياه فمشكل على مذهبه على تقدير أخذ اللزوم التقديري.
Chapter on words. The three indications in relation to each other. Both are entailed by [mutabaqa]: that is, tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa. This is a statement of relations apparent according to the view of the Mīzāniyān and some eminent scholars, and not apparent according to the view of the author. For according to him, iltizam is indication of an external [meaning] in which the word is used metaphorically, and it is not permissible to use one word both literally and metaphorically together, so its indication of what it is placed for does not exist, unless it is said: the entailment is estimative, meaning that there is a meaning such that if the word were used for it, its indication would be mutabaqa. And there is no converse: that is, not every mutabaqa entails tadammun, because there are words placed for simple meanings. And [also not every mutabaqa entails] iltizam, and this is problematic if estimative entailment is intended according to the author's view, because there is no meaning except that it has an external [referent] such that if the word were used for it, its indication would be iltizam. And according to the view of the Mīzāniyān and some eminent scholars it is apparent, because it is possible that a necessary concomitant is placed for it rationally and conventionally. There is no word corresponding to a meaning. And when the Imam claimed that there is no meaning except that it has a rational necessary concomitant, and the least of it is not-other-than-it, he refuted it by saying: (and its being) not-other-than-it is not always what comes to the mind. Understanding it understanding it rationally or conventionally. And as for the tadammunī and iltizāmī [relations], there is no entailment between them. The non-entailment of iltizam by it is apparent, because it is realized in simples as well. And as for the non-entailment of tadammun by it, it is problematic according to his doctrine on the assumption of taking estimative entailment.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion about the relationships among the three types of linguistic indication: مطابقة (literal correspondence), تضمن (inclusion), and التزام (implication). The author begins by stating that both تضمن and التزام necessarily entail مطابقة. This means that if a word indicates a meaning by inclusion or implication, it must also have a literal indication for some meaning. However, the converse is not true: مطابقة does not necessarily entail تضمن or التزام. The author then explains that this relational claim is clear according to the view of the Mīzāniyān (logicians who emphasize the 'criterion') and some eminent scholars, but is not clear according to the view of the author of the original text (likely the matn). The reason is that in the author's view, التزام is indication of an external meaning for which the word is used metaphorically, and a single word cannot be used both literally and metaphorically at the same time. Therefore, a word cannot have both a literal indication (مطابقة) and an implicational indication (التزام) simultaneously. To resolve this, it is said that the entailment is 'estimative' (تقديري): meaning that there exists some meaning such that if the word were used for it, the indication would be مطابقة. The author then clarifies that the lack of converse (that مطابقة does not entail تضمن) is evident because there are words placed for simple meanings that have no parts to include. The lack of entailment of التزام is problematic on the author's own view if estimative entailment is assumed, because every meaning has an external referent such that if the word were used for it, it would be implicational. However, on the view of the Mīzāniyān, it is apparent because a necessary concomitant can be placed rationally or conventionally. The author then discusses the claim of the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) that every meaning has a rational necessary concomitant, the least of which is 'not-other-than-it'. The author refutes this by stating that 'not-other-than-it' is not always what comes to the mind, meaning that it is not a prevalent, well-known necessary concomitant. Finally, the author asserts that there is no entailment between تضمن and التزام: they do not imply each other. The lack of entailment from تضمن to التزام is problematic on the author's view if estimative entailment is assumed, but in general, the passage clarifies the logical dependencies among these three fundamental types of indication.

Key Terms
دلالة مطابقة
دلالة تضمن
دلالة التزام
لزوم تقديري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) No entailment relations exist among them.

Key Takeaway
Tadammun and iltizam always presuppose mutabaqa, but mutabaqa does not always have tadammun or iltizam implications.

Definition and Division of Compound and Single Words
Author defines murakkab and mufrad, then divides mufrad into ism, fi'l, and harf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compound (murakkab) and single (mufrad) words, and identify the three types of single words (ism, fi'l, harf) based on independence.

Core Idea
Compound words indicate meaning for a human self; single words are divided into noun, verb, and particle by independence.

In logic, words are classified by how they convey meaning. The author begins by defining compound words (murakkab) as those that convey a complete meaning for a human self, and single words (mufrad) as those that do not. He then divides single words into three categories: noun (ism), verb (kalima/fi'l), and particle (adaat). The key distinction is whether the word is independent in meaning (istiqlal) or dependent on another word. This classification is essential for building valid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of murakkab and mufrad
Find the sentence where the author says: if a word indicates a meaning for a human self, it is compound (murakkab); otherwise it is single (mufrad). Mark the key terms.
Note that 'compound' here is a logical term, not a grammatical one.
2
Understand the mirror metaphor for mufrad
The author says a single word can be a 'mirror for knowing the state of another' – meaning it indicates a meaning that is not self-standing but relates to something else. This is the criterion for dependency.
This metaphor helps distinguish words like 'min' (from) which only gain meaning in context.
3
Extract the three divisions of mufrad
Read the part where the author lists: particle (adaat), verb (kalima), and noun (ism) as the primary divisions. Note that 'kalima' here specifically means verb, not just any word.
In logic, these three correspond to the three parts of a proposition: subject (ism), predicate (fi'l or ism), and connecting particle.
4
Grasp the concept of independence (istiqlal)
The author explains that some words (like nouns) are fit for judgment and independent in concept, while others (like particles) are not. Independence means the word can be used alone as a subject or predicate.
This distinction is crucial because only independent words can serve as terms in a syllogism.
5
Apply the principle to a compound of independent and dependent parts
The author adds that a compound of independent and non-independent parts may still be considered independently as a whole. This principle is useful in analyzing complex expressions.
Example: 'The king's horse' – 'the king' is independent, 'horse' is independent, but the whole phrase is independent too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لشخص إنساني، فَمُرَكَّبٌ، وَيُسَمَّى قَوْلًا وَمُؤلَّفًا، وَإِلَّا فَمُفْرَدٌ . وَهُوَ إِنْ كَانَ مِرْآةً لِتَعَرُّفِ الْغَيْرِ فَقَط، أي دالا على معنى لا يلاحظ بالذات، بل على أنه من أحوال الغير. تقسيم المفرد وتفصيله أنه كما أن ههنا أوصافا يتعلق بشيء أولا وبالذات، كالقسمة إلى أداة وكلمة واسم، وثانيا وبالعرض للآخر، بحيث يكون الأول واسطة في العروض، كالقسمة للسواد كذلك اللحاظ قد يتعلق ببعض المعاني، كمدلول الابتداء بالذات، والآخر بالعرض، كمدلول (مِنْ) ، فالأول يصلح للحكم عليه وبه، ويستقل بالمفهومية، بخلاف الثاني فلا يستقل ، فالاستقلال وعدمه تابعان اللحاظ ، والمركب من المستقل وغير المستقل لا يجب أن يكون غير مستقل ؛ لأن غير المستقل وإن كان ملاحظا بالتبع، انفرادا، لكن يجوز أن يلاحظ المركب وأجزاؤه حال الاجتماع بالذات من غير تبعية أمر آخر. هذا أصل ينفعك في كثير من المواضع، فَأَدَاةٌ.
For a human self, it is compound (murakkab), and is called a statement (qawl) and composed (mu'allaf); otherwise it is single (mufrad). And if it is a mirror for knowing the state of others only, i.e., indicating a meaning not observed by itself but as a state of something else. The division and detail of the single word: just as here there are attributes that pertain to something primarily and intrinsically, like the division into particle (adaat), verb (kalima), and noun (ism), and secondarily and accidentally to another, such that the first is a medium in the attribute, like the division for blackness—similarly, consideration may attach to some meanings, like the meaning of beginning intrinsically, and another accidentally, like the meaning of (min). So the first is suitable for judgment upon it and by it, and is independent in concept, unlike the second which is not independent. So independence and its opposite follow consideration. And the compound of independent and non-independent does not have to be non-independent, because the non-independent, though considered accidentally, individually, may allow the compound and its parts to be considered together intrinsically without following another matter. This is a principle that benefits you in many places. So it is a particle.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines and divides single words (mufrad) from compound (murakkab) in logic. The author begins by stating that if a word indicates a meaning for a human self (shakhs insani), it is compound (murakkab) and is called a statement (qawl) or composed (mu'allaf); otherwise it is single (mufrad). The single word can be a 'mirror' for knowing the state of another, meaning it indicates a meaning not observed by itself but as an attribute of something else. This is the distinction between independent (mustaqill) and dependent meanings. The author then explains that just as attributes attach to something primarily (like division into particle, verb, noun) or secondarily (like blackness), similarly, consideration (lihaz) can attach to meanings intrinsically or accidentally. For example, the meaning of 'beginning' is intrinsic, while the meaning of the particle 'min' (from) is accidental. The former is fit for judgment and is independent in concept; the latter is not independent. Independence depends on consideration. A compound of independent and non-independent parts may still be considered independently as a whole. This principle is useful in many places. The passage concludes that what is non-independent is a particle (adaat). This is a foundational distinction in Arabic logic for analyzing terms.

Key Takeaway
Single words are divided into noun, verb, and particle; only nouns and verbs are independent in meaning, while particles are dependent, and a compound of both types can still be considered independent as a whole.

What Makes a 'Word' (Kalima) in Logic?
Defines the logical term 'kalima' and distinguishes it from the Arabic verb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an Arabic verb (fi'l) and a logical word (kalima) based on their capacity for truth and falsity.

Core Idea
Not every verb is a word: logicians reserve 'kalima' for terms that cannot be true or false by themselves.

In this passage, the author defines the logical term 'kalima' and clarifies a common confusion: why some Arabic verbs are not considered 'words' in logic. Understanding this distinction is essential for building accurate propositions in syllogistic reasoning. The key is that a logical word must be a simple term, not a complete proposition that can be assessed for truth or falsity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of kalima
The author states that a kalima indicates a single independent meaning composed of event, time, and relation. These three elements are analyzed by the mind but do not stand alone.
This is a technical definition specific to logic.
2
Find the key condition
A kalima must not be capable of truth or falsity by itself. This is the decisive criterion that separates logical words from propositions.
Propositions (qadiyya) can be true or false; simple terms cannot.
3
Compare 'amshi' and 'tamshi' with 'yamshi'
'Amshi' (I walk) and 'tamshi' (you walk) are verbs in Arabic grammar, but they contain an implicit subject (I, you), making them complete propositions. Therefore they are not logical words. 'Yamshi' (he walks) lacks a specific subject; it requires an explicit agent (e.g., Zayd) to form a proposition. Hence it is a kalima.
The distinction hinges on whether the expression includes an implicit subject.
4
Test the rule with other verbs
Apply the condition: 'kataba' (he wrote) – no specific subject, so it's a kalima. 'Katabtu' (I wrote) – contains 'tu' (I), so it's a proposition. 'Takubuna' (you all write) – includes subject pronoun, so proposition.
Practice with past tense forms as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الكلمة تدل على معنى واحد إجمالي مستقل، يحلله العقل إلى الحدث والزمان والنسبة العديمي الاستقلال، على ما يشهد به الوجدان. وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ فِعْلِ عِنْدَ الْعَرَبِ كَلِمَةَ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ؛ فَإِنَّ الفعل العربي نَحْوَ أَمْشِي وَتَمْشِي مَثَلًا فِعْلٌ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَلَيْسَ بِكَلِمَةٍ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ، لِاحْتِمَالِهِ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ. بِخِلَافِ يَمْشِي، فَإِنَّهُ كَلِمَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ وَالْعَرَبِ؛ إذْ لا يَصْلُحُ لِلدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ بِنَفْسِهِ، بَلْ يَحْتَاجُ إلَى فَاعِلٍ مُظْهَرٍ.
Know that the word (kalima) indicates a single independent holistic meaning, which the mind analyzes into the event, the time, and the relation, which lack independence, according to what conscience witnesses. And not every verb according to the Arabs is a word according to the logicians; for the Arabic verb like 'I walk' and 'you walk' for example is a verb according to them, but it is not a word according to the logicians, because it can be true or false. Contrary to 'he walks', for it is a word according to the logicians and the Arabs; since it is not suitable by itself to indicate the subject of judgment, but rather needs an explicit agent.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'kalima' (word) and clarifies its relation to the Arabic verb. The author begins by giving the technical definition: kalima is a single independent expression that conveys a composite meaning — an event, a time, and a relation. The mind analyzes it into these three components, but they do not stand alone. Then the author makes a crucial distinction: not every Arabic verb qualifies as a kalima in logic. For example, the present tense forms for the speaker (amshi) or addressee (tamshi) are considered verbs by grammarians but not words by logicians. Why? Because they contain an implicit subject (I, you), and thus they can be true or false — they are complete propositions (qadiyya). In logic, a kalima must be a simple term, not a proposition. In contrast, the third person masculine singular form yamshi is accepted as a kalima by both grammarians and logicians. Its prefix (ya) does not specify a particular agent; it merely indicates an absent male. Therefore yamshi by itself does not form a complete judgment — it requires an explicit subject (e.g., Zayd) to become a proposition. This distinction is essential for understanding how logicians analyze statements for validity and truth in syllogistic reasoning.

فعل

The Unique Property of Nouns: Judgment on Meaning
Author explains the unique property of nouns: judgment on meaning, not just sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between judgment on sound and judgment on meaning, and identify the specific property of nouns in logical analysis.

Core Idea
A noun's unique logical property is that judgment can be made upon its meaning, not just its sound.

You have learned that nouns, verbs, and particles are the three parts of speech in Arabic. But what makes a noun special from a logical perspective? In this lesson, we explore a fundamental distinction: between judging the sound of a word (e.g., 'daraba is a past tense verb') and judging its meaning. The author argues that the unique property of nouns is that we can make judgments on their meanings, a capacity not shared by verbs or particles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of judgment
Notice the author distinguishes between 'judgment upon the sound itself' (الحكم على نفس الصوت) and 'judgment upon its meaning' (الحكم على معناه). Understand that the first applies to any word's linguistic classification, while the second applies only to nouns in a specific way.
2
Recognize the objection
The author anticipates an objection: people make judgments on verbs and particles too, e.g., 'min is a particle' or 'daraba is a past tense verb'. Identify why this might seem to challenge the claim that judgment on meaning is unique to nouns.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author argues that such judgments are on the sound of the word, not its meaning. For example, 'daraba is a past tense verb' judges the grammatical form, not the action of striking. Grasp the distinction: classifying a word grammatically is judgment on sound; predicating something of the thing named is judgment on meaning.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Practice by classifying statements: 'Zaid is standing' judges the meaning of Zaid, while 'Zaid is a noun' judges the sound. Test the author's claim that only nouns can be judged upon in the meaning sense, and note the nuance about nominal concepts.
The author adds that judgment on meaning is specific to nouns only when the noun is not taken as a nominal concept itself (i.e., when it is a mirror to its meaning).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ اسْمٌ، وَمِنْ خواصه عليه الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ، وَقَوْلُهُمْ مِنْ حَرْفٌ جَرَّ وضَرَبَ فِعْلٌ مَاضٍ لَا يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ نَقْضَا، فَإِنَّهُ حُكْمٌ عَلَى نَفْسِ الصَّوْتِ، لا عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ، وَالْمُخْتَصُّ بِهِ هُوَ هَذَا، أي الحكم على معناه؛ إذ يوجد فيه ولا يوجد في غيره، لكن لا مطلقا، بل إذا لم يتصور بعنوان مفهوم اسمي جعل مرآة لملاحظته، وأما إذا جعل عنوانه مفهوما اسميا فيحكم عليه، فلا يرد معنى من غير مستقل، ولا يحتاج إلى هذا العذر الحاكم على الطبيعة في المحصورات أو ما في قوتها، بل الحاكم على الأفراد، وَالْأَوَّلُ يجري في الْمُهْمَلَاتِ أَيْضًا نَحْوُ جسق مُهْمَلٌ.
And if it is not (indicative of time by its form) then it is a noun, and among its properties is that judgment is made upon it. And their saying 'min is a particle' and 'daraba is a past tense verb' is not a refutation against it, for that is a judgment upon the sound itself, not upon its meaning. And the specific property (of the noun) is this: judgment upon its meaning, for it exists in the noun and does not exist in other parts of speech. But not absolutely; rather when it is not conceived under the aspect of a nominal concept made a mirror for its observation. But if its aspect is made a nominal concept, then judgment is made upon it. So the meaning of (the word) 'min' is not independent, and does not need this excuse. The one who judges upon the nature in limited individuals or those in their power actually judges upon the individuals. And the first (type of judgment, i.e., upon the sound) also applies to meaningless words, such as 'jasaq' is meaningless.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the unique property of the noun (ism) in contrast to other parts of speech. The author begins by stating the rule: if a word's form does not indicate time, it is a noun. Among the properties of a noun is that a judgment (hukm) can be made upon it. He then anticipates an objection: one might say that judgments are also made upon verbs and particles, e.g., saying 'min is a particle' or 'daraba is a past tense verb'. The author refutes this by distinguishing two types of judgment: judgment upon the sound (nafs al-sawt) and judgment upon the meaning (ma'na). The examples given are judgments on the sound of the word, not on its meaning. The specific property of the noun is judgment upon its meaning; this exists in nouns and not in other parts of speech. However, he qualifies this: judgment on the meaning is specific to nouns only when the noun is not conceived under a nominal concept that makes it a mirror for observation. In other words, when we take the noun as a word and not as a concept, we judge its meaning. But if we take the nominal concept itself (e.g., 'the word Zaid'), then judgment is upon that concept, not the meaning. He then clarifies that the meaning of a particle like 'min' is not independent, so it cannot be judged upon in the same way. Finally, he notes that judgment upon the sound applies even to meaningless words (muhmalat) like 'jasaq'. This distinction is crucial in logic for understanding how terms function in propositions.

Key Takeaway
The specific property of nouns is that judgment can be made upon their meaning, not merely their sound, and this distinguishes them from verbs and particles.

Two Objections on Intensity and Weakness in Attributes
Author presents two objections and Bahr al-Ulum's reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that intensity/weakness are accidental, not essential, by analyzing the ground of predication (miṣdāq).

Core Idea
The ground of predication determines whether an attribute can vary in intensity without changing the essence.

In this passage, the author presents two objections to the Peripatetic view that intensity and weakness are accidental attributes. The commentator Bahr al-Ulum then offers a reconciliation based on the concept of miṣdāq (ground of predication). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the difference between essential and accidental predication in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Peripatetic claims being attacked
The Peripatetics hold that intensity/weakness in attributes (e.g., whiteness) are due to accidents associated with the attribute, not the essence itself. The author objects to this.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection states that even if the intense instance has an accident absent in the weak, that accident does not prove the intensity is accidental. The essence itself could be intense, and the accident only serves as a middle term in proof.
Consider the example of whiteness: intense whiteness might have a tinge not present in weak whiteness, but that does not mean whiteness itself is accidental.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection argues that the lack of a difference in terms of having or lacking an accident does not mean there is no difference at all. The difference could be in the mode of existence (nahw min al-wujud) of the essence itself.
For instance, an essence might exist in one mode as intense and in another as weak, without any additional accident.
4
Analyze Bahr al-Ulum's reconciliation
Bahr al-Ulum interprets intensity as the predication of a universal to one subject with many grounds of predication (miṣdāq). For essential attributes, the miṣdāq is the subject itself, so it cannot multiply without multiplying the subject. For accidents, the miṣdāq is a mental principle (mabda') that can multiply, allowing intensity.
This makes the dispute verbal: once the concept of miṣdāq is clarified, both parties can agree, but the Illuminationist position (that essence itself can be intense) is the correct literal reading.
5
Determine the conclusion
The passage concludes that the truth is with the Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun), and the Peripatetics' statement can only be correct through a reinterpretation.
The author sides with the Illuminationists, highlighting that the logical analysis of predication grounds resolves the apparent conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثانيا : فلأن اشتمال الشديد على عارض ليس في الضعيف لا يوجب التشكيك في العارض فقط، بل يجوز أن يكون الماهية بنفسها شديدة أو ضعيفة، ويكون العارض واسطة في الثبوت فقط ، فتأمل فيهما. وأما ثالثا فلأن عدم اشتمال الشديد والزائد على أمر ليس في الناقص والضعيف لا يوجب عدم الفرق بينهما ؛ لجواز أن يكون الفرق بنحو من الوجود، فيكون الماهية بنفسها في نحو من الوجود زائدة أو شديدة، وفي نحو آخر ناقصة أو ضعيفة. ثم إنه قال الجامع بين المعقول والمنقول، بحر العلوم، صاحب المقامات الرفيعة أبونا وأستاذنا نظام الملة والدين، أغرقه الله في بحار رحمته وأسكنه في مقام خلته في تعليقاته على الحواشي القديمة: يمكن توجيه كلام المشائيين بأن الشدة صدق الكلي على موضوع واحد بأصداق كثيرة، والضعف ضده، وذا لا يمكن في الذاتي ؛ فإن الصدق لا يتكثر إلا بتكثر المصداق، ولو تكثر المصداق في صدق الذاتي لتكثر ذات الموضوع ؛ لأنها المصداق، هذا خلف، بخلاف العارض؛ إذ المصداق فيه هو المبدأ القائم بالموضوع، فإذا كان شديدا أمكن انتزاع أمثال الضعيف، فيتكرر الحمل بحسبها، ثم قال: وعلى هذا يصير النزاع بين الفريقين لفظيا. وقال أيضا: ويأبى عنه كلمات المشائين أيضا، وقد ظهر لك من هذا القيل والقال أن الحق ما قال الإشراقية، ولا يصح قول المشائين إلا بالتأويل بنحو آخر، والله أعلم بالصواب.
As for the second: because the intense having an accident that the weak lacks does not necessitate that the intensity is only in the accident, rather it is possible that the essence itself is intense or weak, and the accident is only a middle term in proof. Then the third: because the intense and the superior lacking something that the deficient and weak do not have does not necessitate that there is no difference between them; because it is possible that the difference is in a mode of existence, such that the essence itself in one mode of existence is superior or intense, and in another mode is deficient or weak. Then the compiler of the rational and transmitted, Bahr al-Ulum, the possessor of lofty stations, our father and our teacher Niẓām al-Milla wa al-Din, may Allah drown him in the seas of His mercy and lodge him in the station of His love, said in his annotations on the old glosses: The speech of the Peripatetics can be interpreted that intensity is the predication of a universal to one subject with many grounds of predication, and weakness is its opposite, and this is not possible in the essential; because predication only multiplies by the multiplication of the predication-ground, and if the predication-ground multiplies in the predication of the essential, then the subject's essence would multiply because it is the predication-ground, which is a contradiction. Unlike the accident, because its predication-ground is the principle subsisting in the subject, so if it is intense, it is possible to extract instances of the weak, so the predication repeats accordingly. Then he said: On this basis, the dispute between the two groups becomes verbal. He also said: And the words of the Peripatetics reject it as well. It has become clear to you from this discussion that the truth is what the Illuminationists said, and the Peripatetics' statement cannot be correct except through another interpretation. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Peripatetic (Mashsha'i) view that intensity and weakness (shidda/da'f) are accidental differences, not essential. The first objection states that even if in an intense instance of an attribute (e.g., intense whiteness) there is an accident not found in the weak (e.g., whiteness with a tinge), that accident does not cause the intensity; rather the essence itself could be intense, and the accident only serves as middle term in proof. The second objection states that the difference between intense and weak may be in mode of existence, not in essence. The author then quotes Bahr al-Ulum (the commentator) who offers a reconciliation: intensity is the predication of a universal on a single subject with multiple grounds of predication (i.e., multiple instantiations of a meaning). This cannot occur in essential attributes because essential predication requires the subject itself as ground, which cannot multiply without multiplying the subject. For accidents, the ground is a mental principle that can have multiple intensities. This makes the dispute verbal. The commentator concludes that the Illuminationists' position is correct, while the Peripatetics' requires reinterpretation.

Key Terms
التشكيك (al-tashkik): The doctrine that a concept applies to its instances in different degrees (intensity/weakness) by essence, not by accident. [Note: This term is central in Illuminationist ontology; Peripatetics typically deny tashkik in essential attributes.]
العارض (al-'āriḍ): An accidental attribute that is not part of the essence, here distinguished from essential (dhāti). [Note: No significant shift.]
المصداق (al-miṣdāq): The ground or referent of predication; the thing that makes a universal true of a particular. [Note: In logic texts, miṣdāq often refers to the external referent; here it is the ground of truth.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's interpretation, why can intensity/weakness not occur in essential (dhāti) attributes?
A) Because essential attributes always have a single ground of predication, which cannot multiply without multiplying the subject.
B) Because essences are simple and cannot admit of degrees.
C) Because essential attributes are not subject to variation in individuals.
D) Because contraries cannot coexist in the same subject.

Key Takeaway
Intensity and weakness can only be accidental, not essential, because the ground of predication for essential attributes is the subject itself, which cannot multiply without changing the essence.

The Second Disagreement: Intense and Weak Qualities – A Dialectical Analysis
Dialectical exchange on the essence of intense and weak qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between the Illuminationists and Peripatetics on whether intense and weak qualities share the same essence, and identify the logical resolution.

Core Idea
Intense and weak qualities: same essence or different? The debate hinges on continuity and infinite gradation.

In this passage, we encounter a classic dialectical exchange between two schools of Islamic philosophy: the Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun) and the Peripatetics (Masha'un). The question at stake is whether a quality in its intense and weak degrees (e.g., deep black vs. faint black) shares a single essence or differs in essence. The Illuminationists argue for unity, citing the continuous nature of qualitative change; the Peripatetics counter with a clever analogical reductio involving a descending chain of blacks. The author then resolves the dispute by pointing out an assumption that makes the Peripatetic argument impossible. By dissecting this exchange, you will learn how to analyze dialectical arguments and assess analogical reasoning in an Islamic philosophical context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Locate the claim of the Illuminationists (that intense and weak are united in essence) and the claim of the Peripatetics (that they differ in essence). Note the different methods of proof: the Illuminationists appeal to self-evidence and continuous motion; the Peripatetics use an analogical reductio.
The Illuminationists' first argument: motion in quality requires a single non-static quality. Second: whiteness in a continuous body varies in intensity but all parts are whiteness.
2
Extract the Peripatetic analogical argument
The Peripatetics propose a thought experiment: imagine a series of decreasing blacks (each half the intensity of the previous) leading to pure whiteness. They argue that if each black in the series shares one essence, then by transitivity the end term (whiteness) would also be black—an absurd conclusion. This shows the initial assumption (that blacks share essence) is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum using analogy: the relationship between successive blacks is analogous to that between the weakest black and whiteness.
3
Analyze the refutation by the author
The author notes that the Peripatetic argument is refuted by quantity (al-miqdar) because the same premises would apply to continuous quantities, which are agreed to be homogeneous. The real solution: qualities in intensity are not bounded to a finite limit; the assumption of a descending series ending in whiteness is impossible—just as quantities are infinitely divisible.
The author's point is that the Peripatetic's thought experiment assumes a finite limit to the series, but qualities can be infinitesimally graded, so the series never reaches a boundary.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
Determine which side has the stronger argument after the resolution. The Illuminationists' position is upheld because the Peripatetic objection is shown to rely on an impossible premise. However, the passage leaves open the deeper philosophical question of whether essences are truly identical or merely similar.
In dialectical terms, the Illuminationists defend their claim successfully by exposing the flaw in the counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الثاني قال الإشراقيون: الشديد والضعيف متحدان بالماهية، ويدعون فيه البديهة، وهو أجدر بذلك، وبعد التنزل يستدل بأن الحركة في الكيف يستدعي أن يكون كيف واحد غير قار موجودا بأن المبدأ والمنتهى، كما تقرر في الطبعيات، فلا بد من أن يكون أجزاؤه متحدة الماهية، وبعضها شديدة وبعضها ضعيفة. وأيضا البياض القائم بالجسم المتصل بعضه شديد وبعضه ضعيف، فلا بد من كونهما متفقي الماهية؛ لكونهما جزء في المتصل. قال المشاؤون : إنهما مختلفان بالماهية، وأوثق شبهاتهم إن فرضنا سوادات متناقصة، بأن يكون السواد الأول ضعف الثاني والثاني ضعف الثالث، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهي إلى البياض الحق، فنسبته إلى السواد الأضعف الذي تحته كنسبة هذا السواد إلى الأشد الذي فوقه، فلو كانت السوادات متفقة الماهية كان هو والبياض أيضا كذلك، هذا خلف، وهو منقوض بالمقدار؛ لجريان مقدمات الدليل فيه. والحل أن الكيفيات في الشدة والضعف غير منتهية إلى حد، فذلك الفرض محال، كما في الكميات.
The second disagreement: The Illuminationists said: The intense and the weak are united in essence, and they claim self-evidence for that, and it is more deserving. And after conceding, they infer that motion in quality requires that there be a single non-static quality existing between the beginning and end, as established in natural philosophy. So its parts must be united in essence, some intense and some weak. Also, whiteness subsisting in a continuous body: part of it is intense and part weak, so they must be identical in essence because they are part of the continuous. The Peripatetics said: They differ in essence, and their strongest proof is that if we assume decreasing blacks, such that the first black is half of the second, and the second half of the third, and so on until it ends in pure whiteness, then the ratio of this black to the weaker black below it is like the ratio of this black to the stronger above it. So if the blacks were identical in essence, then whiteness would also be so, which is absurd. This refutation is itself refuted by quantity, because the same premises apply to it. The solution is that qualities in intensity and weakness are not limited to a limit, so that assumption is impossible, as in quantities.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the second disagreement regarding whether intense and weak instances of a quality (e.g., intense black vs. weak black) share the same essence. The Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun) claim they are identical in essence, appealing to self-evidence. Their proof: motion in quality (e.g., a gradual change from one shade to another) requires a continuous process whose parts are of the same nature but differ in intensity. Also, in a continuous body, whiteness varies in intensity across its extension, yet all parts are whiteness—so they must share the same essence. The Peripatetics counter with their strongest argument: imagine a series of decreasing blacks (e.g., each successive black is half the intensity of the previous), leading to pure whiteness. By analogy, if each black shares the same essence, then by transitivity, whiteness would also share that essence—which is false. Thus, blacks differ in essence. The author then notes that this argument is refuted by quantity (al-miqdar): the same reasoning would apply to continuous quantities (e.g., lines), which are generally agreed to be homogeneous. The solution is that qualities in intensity/weakness are not bounded to a finite limit; the assumption of a finite descending series leading to whiteness is invalid—just as quantities can be infinitely divisible.

Key Terms
الحركة في الكيف
التناقص
الكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the same reasoning would apply to continuous quantities, which are homogeneous.
C) Because the series cannot start from a first black.
D) Because the Illuminationists provided a stronger self-evident proof.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical exchanges, an argument that proves too much (e.g., implying quantities are not homogeneous) can be refuted by pointing out that its premises lead to a conclusion that contradicts accepted knowledge.

Defining Intensity and Weakness: Conceptual Extraction
Author explains the definition of intensity as conceptual extraction of weaker instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Dawwānī's definition of intensity as conceptual extraction of weaker instances and distinguish it from the Illuminationist view.

Core Idea
Intensity means the mind extracts weaker instances from the stronger, creating the illusion of composition.

This passage tackles a key debate in Islamic logic: how to define intensity (shidda) and weakness (da'f) when essences admit gradation. The Illuminationists defined it as perfection of the essence itself, but the Peripatetic scholar al-Dawwānī offered a refined definition in terms of conceptual analysis. Understanding this definition helps you grasp how logicians handle gradation without resorting to real composition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Terms
Identify the key terms: shidda (intensity), da'f (weakness), tashkik (gradation), and their role in essence discussions.
Tashkik refers to the idea that a single essence can be instantiated in varying degrees.
2
Identify the Illuminationist Position
The Illuminationists (ishrāqiyyūn) held that intensity is the perfection (kamāl) of the essence itself, and weakness its imperfection, with effects being more numerous in intense instances.
They allowed tashkik directly in the essence (māhiyya).
3
Analyze al-Dawwānī's Alternative
Al-Dawwānī redefined intensity as the mind (al-'aql), with the help of estimation (al-wahm), extracting similar instances of the weaker from the intense and analyzing it into them. This is a conceptual operation, not real composition.
He follows the Peripatetic view that gradation is in accidents, not essence.
4
Recognize the Common Misunderstanding
Some may think the intense is actually composed of weaker parts. The author clarifies this is an illusion of the common estimation, not a real metaphysical claim.
The term 'fafham' emphasizes grasping this subtlety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الثالث لما جوز الاشراقية التشكيك في الماهية فسروا الشدة والضعف بكمال نفس الماهية وضعفها وهو متصور على أنحاء قد يكون بحيث يكون آثار الماهية أكثر، وقد يكون بحيث ينتزع عنه الوهم أمثال الأضعف، والمحقق الدواني لما خالفهم اتباعا للمشائين غير التفسير، وقال كما أشار إليه المصنف : (وَمَعْنى كَوْنِ أَحَدِ الْفَرْدَيْنِ أَشَدَّ أَنَّهُ بِحَيْثُ يَنْتَزِعُ مِنْهُ الْعَقْلُ بِمَعُونَةِ الْوَهْمِ أَمْثَالَ الْأَضْعَفِ ، وَيُحَلَّلْهُ [الْعَقْلُ] إِلَيْهَا)، أي إلى أمثال الأضعف، (حَتىَ إِنَّ الْأَوْهَامَ الْعَامَّةَ تَذْهَبُ إِلَى أَنَّهُ) ، أي الأشد (مُتَألّف مِنْهَا) ، أمثال الأضعف، (فَافُهُمْ). وفسره بعضهم بكثرة آثار الماهية، وهذا أيضا مما يناسب مذهب المشائين.
The third disagreement: When the Illuminationists allowed tashkik in essence, they interpreted intensity and weakness as perfection and imperfection of the essence itself. This is conceived in many ways: sometimes the effects of the essence are more numerous, and sometimes the mind extracts weak instances from it. Al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī, when he opposed them following the Peripatetics, changed the interpretation and said, as the author indicated: 'The meaning of one of the two individuals being more intense is that the mind, with the help of the imagination, extracts similar instances of the weaker from it, and the mind analyzes it into them', i.e., into instances of the weaker, 'so that the common imaginations go so far as to think that it (the intense) is composed of them', i.e., of instances of the weaker. So understand. And some interpreted it as the abundance of the effects of the essence, and this also suits the Peripatetic doctrine.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the third disagreement concerning the definition of intensity and weakness (shidda/da'f). The Illuminationists, who allow tashkik (gradation) in essence, define intensity as the perfection (kamāl) of the essence itself, and weakness as its imperfection. They conceive this in ways: either the essence has more effects (i.e., stronger instantiation) or the mind (al-wahm) can extract weaker instances from it. The scholar al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī, following the Peripatetics, modifies the definition: intensity means that from the intense individual, the intellect (al-'aql) with the help of estimation (al-wahm) extracts similar instances of the weaker, and analyzes the intense into those weaker instances—to the extent that common estimation thinks the intense is composed of those weaker parts. This is a conceptual analysis, not real composition. The author notes that another interpretation defines intensity as the abundance of effects (āthār) of the essence, which also aligns with the Peripatetic view that intensity is accidental, not essential. The passage ends with 'fafham' (so understand), emphasizing the point.

Key Terms
الوهم
التحليل

Key Takeaway
Al-Dawwānī's definition of intensity reframes gradation as a conceptual operation of the mind, not a real composition of essence.

The Identity of Increase, Potency, and Intensity
Author defines three terms and critiques Mashshaiyun's distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Illuminationist view that increase, potency, and intensity are one reality differentiated only by category, and identify the Peripatetic contradiction regarding length.

Core Idea
One reality, three names: same perfection appears in quantity, substance, and quality.

Our passage tackles a subtle but critical point: Are terms like 'increase,' 'potency,' and 'intensity' referring to three different things or one thing with different names? The Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun) argue for unity, while the Peripatetics (Mashsha'un) treat them as distinct. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to distinguish between linguistic convention and philosophical reality, a skill vital for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms and their substrates
In the Arabic text, find the three terms: الزيادة (increase), القوة (potency), and الشدة (intensity). Note the categories they are linked to: quantity, substance, and quality respectively. Write down the pairing: increase = quantity, potency = substance, intensity = quality.
These pairings are explicitly given in the passage.
2
Understand the Illuminationist claim of unity
The Illuminationists assert that these three are 'one thing, the perfection of quiddity' (أمر واحد، كمال الماهية). The difference in name is purely due to the category in which this perfection appears. Consider this like water being called 'ice' when solid and 'steam' when gas—same substance, different names based on state.
The passage says 'but when found in quantity it is called increase...' indicating the name depends on the substrate.
3
Recognize the Peripatetic error and their self-contradiction
The Peripatetics, relying on everyday language, thought these terms refer to distinct realities. The author points out their inconsistency: they deny that a line can be 'more intensely a line' (أشد خطية) but allow it to be 'more intense in length' (أشد طولا), even though length is the line. This shows they treat the same reality differently depending on terminology.
The contradiction lies in treating 'line' and 'length' as different when they are the same thing.
4
See how the author resolves the issue
The author (of Bahr Ulum) adopts the Illuminationist position, defining intensity broadly to include increase. This resolution respects the philosophical identity while acknowledging the conventional distinctions. Reflect on how this approach avoids multiplying entities unnecessarily.
The passage ends: 'the author chose the doctrine of the Illuminationists, since he defined intensity as including increase as well.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الرابع: قال الإشراقيون الزيادة والقوة والشدة أمر واحد، كمال الماهية، لكن إذا وجدت في الكم سميت زيادة، وإذا وجدت في الجوهر سميت قوة، وإذا وجدت في الكيف سميت شدة، وكذا أضدادها، لكن هذه إطلاقات عرفية لا اعتداد بها في العلوم الحكمية، والمشاؤون زلوا وقالوا بتغايرها؛ نظرا إلى الإطلاقات، وناقضوا أنفسهم حيث لم يجوزوا كون الخط أشد خطية، وجوزوا كونه أشد طولا، مع أن الطول هو الخط، والمصنف اختار مذهب الإشراقية؛ حيث عرف الشدة بما يتناول الزيادة أيضا.
The fourth difference: The Illuminationists said increase, potency, and intensity are one thing, the perfection of quiddity, but when found in quantity it is called increase, when found in substance it is called potency, and when found in quality it is called intensity, and likewise their opposites. However, these are conventional usages not considered in the philosophical sciences. The Peripatetics erred and said they are distinct, looking at the usages, and contradicted themselves when they did not allow a line to be more intensely a line but allowed it to be more intense in length, while length is the line. And the author chose the doctrine of the Illuminationists, since he defined intensity as including increase as well.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines a philosophical difference regarding terminology. The Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun) assert that increase (ziyada), potency (quwwa), and intensity (shidda) are identical in reality—they are the perfection (kamal) of a quiddity (mahiyya). The difference in name arises only from the substrate: when found in quantity it is called increase, in substance potency, in quality intensity. The Peripatetics (Mashsha'un) mistakenly considered them distinct due to everyday language, and contradicted themselves by allowing a line to be more intense in length but not more intensely a line, even though length is the line. The author (of Bahr Ulum) sides with the Illuminationists, defining intensity broadly to encompass increase. This highlights the philosophical principle that terms used in different categories may refer to the same reality.

القوة
الشدة
الإشراقيون
المشاؤون
كمال الماهية

B) They claimed increase and potency are identical.
C) They disagreed with the Illuminationists on the definition of quiddity.
D) They used conventional language in philosophical sciences.

Key Takeaway
Increase, potency, and intensity are not three distinct things but one perfection of quiddity named according to the category (quantity, substance, quality) in which it appears.

8 min
Debate on Intensification of Substance: Illuminationists vs Peripatetics
Debate on whether substance can undergo intensification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Illuminationist and Peripatetic positions on the intensification of substance and identify the key argument each side uses.

Core Idea
Substance can intensify if quiddity perfection is the criterion.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth disagreement concerning whether substance (jawhar) can undergo intensification (ishtidad). The Illuminationists affirm it based on the perfection of quiddity, while the Peripatetics deny it relying on linguistic convention. Understanding this debate clarifies how different schools define substance and intensity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Locate the Arabic phrase 'هل الجوهر يشتد' and note the two opposing answers: Illuminationists say yes, Peripatetics say no.
2
Understand the Illuminationist position
They define intensity as perfection of quiddity (kamaal al-mahiya). Example: an elephant's substance-quiddity is more perfect than a mosquito's due to greater effects. They also claim verification through spiritual exercises.
This is based on their principle that intensity applies to quiddity itself, not just accidents.
3
Understand the Peripatetic position
They deny intensification of substance without a logical proof, relying on ordinary language: we do not say 'more intense' for substances. However, the author notes that their own principle admits increase and decrease in substance because bodily quantity (a substance) exhibits these.
This creates an inconsistency in their position.
4
Evaluate the arguments
Assess the strength: Illuminationists provide an example and an appeal to empirical observation (spiritual exercises). Peripatetics rely on convention, which may not be a strong logical basis.
The author seems to favor the Illuminationist view by pointing out the weakness in the Peripatetic argument.
5
Test your understanding
Apply the Illuminationist criterion to another pair: can the substance of fire be considered more intense than that of water? Consider the manifestations (effects) of each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الخامس أن الجوهر هل يشتد، أم لا؟ قال الإشراقيون: نعم، وهو ظاهر على أصلهم؛ فإنهم عنوا بها كمال الماهية، والماهية الجوهرية في الفيل أكمل من البعوضة؛ لظهور الآثار الكثيرة في الفيل دونها، وعلى ما فسره أتباع المشائين له تجويز أيضا؛ فإن من الجائز أن يكون بعض المفارقات بحيث ينتزع عنه أمثال مفارق آخر، ولم يدل دليل على خلافه، وقد ادعى الإشراقيون فيه المشاهدة بالرياضيات، وقال المشاؤون: لا يشتد الجوهر، ولم أنه يقيموا عليه دليلا بل بنوا على مجرى العرف؛ حيث لم يطلق على جوهر أشد من جوهر آخر، هذا والله أعلم بالصواب. والزيادة والنقصان على أصلهم أيضا يتصف الجوهر بهما فإنهم قالوا المقدار الجسماني جوهر وعين الجسم، مع أنه يتصف بهما، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال.
The fifth difference: Does substance intensify or not? The Illuminationists said: Yes, and it is evident according to their principle; for they meant by it the perfection of quiddity, and the substance-quiddity in an elephant is more perfect than that of a mosquito, due to the appearance of many effects in the elephant without it. And according to what the followers of the Peripatetics explained, there is also possibility; for it is possible that some separate entities are such that the likes of another separate entity are abstracted from it, and no proof indicates contrary. And the Illuminationists claimed in this matter observation through spiritual exercises. The Peripatetics said: Substance does not intensify, and they did not establish a proof for it but based it on the flow of convention, since they did not say of one substance that it is more intense than another. This, and God knows best what is correct. And increase and decrease, according to their principle, also characterize substance; for they said that bodily quantity is a substance and identical to the body, while it is characterized by them, and God knows best the reality of the state.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth disagreement concerning whether substance (jawhar) can undergo intensification (ishtidad). The Illuminationists (Ishraqiyun) affirm it, as it aligns with their definition of intensity as perfection of quiddity. They illustrate by comparing an elephant and a mosquito: the substance-quiddity of an elephant is more perfect due to greater manifestations. They also grant possibility from a Peripatetic standpoint: separate entities could potentially yield abstraction of another separate entity, with no proof against it. The Illuminationists even claim verification through spiritual exercises. The Peripatetics, however, deny intensification of substance, not with logical proof but based on ordinary language where one says 'more intense' only for qualities, not substances. The author then adds a note that on the Peripatetics' own principle, substance actually does admit increase and decrease because they consider bodily quantity as substance yet it undeniably increases and decreases. This undermines their position. The passage thus clarifies the conceptions of intensity and substance in the two schools.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الاشتداد
المفارقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example do the Illuminationists use to show intensification of substance?
A) A stone becoming harder.
B) An elephant's substance being more perfect than a mosquito's.
C) Fire burning more fiercely.
D) A body gaining weight.

Key Takeaway
The debate on intensification of substance hinges on whether intensity is defined as quiddity perfection (Illuminationist) or is restricted by linguistic convention (Peripatetic).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Based on the Illuminationist definition of intensity as perfection of quiddity, argue whether the substance of a human being is more intense than that of a stone. Use the example of manifestations (effects) as evidence.
?
2. The Peripatetics deny substance intensification based on linguistic convention. Provide a counterexample from fiqh where convention is overturned by rational proof, showing the weakness of their position.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
Defining Mushtarak (Homonym) in Arabic Logic
Defines mushtarak and discusses its occurrence and usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given word qualifies as a mushtarak according to the criteria of multiple independent coinings without transfer.

Core Idea
A homonym is a single word coined separately for each distinct meaning, not a transferred term.

In logic and usul al-fiqh, the concept of mushtarak (homonym) is essential for understanding how a single word can carry multiple independent meanings. This passage defines mushtarak by contrasting it with other types of polysemy like transferred terms (al-manqul) and personal pronouns. Let us break down the definition and its implications for Islamic textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining conditions
Read the Arabic definition: 'if that single word is placed for each of the meanings with multiple placements ... then it is a homonym.' Note that mushtarak requires multiple separate coinings (awdaa) for each meaning.
The word 'wad'' (coinage) is key: each meaning has its own act of assignment.
2
Distinguish mushtarak from similar categories
The passage excludes 'transferred terms' (manqul) where a word is first coined for one meaning then moved to another, and also excludes personal pronouns like 'ana' (I) which refer to a single meaning (the speaker) despite multiple referents.
In usul al-fiqh, distinguishing mushtarak from manqul is crucial for determining legal interpretations.
3
Examine the occurrence and example
The author affirms that mushtarak does occur in Arabic and even in the Quran, giving the example of 'qur'' meaning both menstruation and purity. This shows that homonyms can even have opposite meanings.
The example of 'qur'' is from Surah al-Baqarah 2:228 and is a classic case in Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Analyze the controversy on simultaneous meaning
The passage notes a disagreement: the author's school (likely Hanafi) holds that using mushtarak for all its meanings simultaneously as real (not metaphorical) is permissible, while al-Shafi'i reportedly denies this. Understand the basis of each position.
This relates to the principle of 'istihsan' and different approaches to ambiguous language in legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كثر معناه، فإن وضع ذلك المفرد لكل من المعاني بأوضاع، فيخرج نحو أنا ابتداء، بلا تخلل نقل فمشترك. فصل في الألفاظ بأوضاع، فيخرج نحو أنا ابتداء، بلا تخلل نقل فمشترك. اختلف تقسيم الاسم الكثير المعنى ومنقول وحقيقة أولا في إمكان المشترك، ثم في وقوعه، ثم في كونه للضدين، والحق عند إلى مشترك الجماهير أي المشترك واقع في كلام العرب، بل في كلام الله تعالى، حتى بين الضدين، كالقرء للحيض والطهر، لكن لا عموم فيه، وهو ههنا إرادة كل معانيه حقيقة عندنا، خلافا للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى، وأما إرادة جميع معانيه على أنه معنى واحد مجازي، أو أحد، ومجاز
And if its meaning becomes multiple, then if that single word is placed for each of the meanings with multiple placements, excluding something like 'I' as a beginning, without an intervening transfer, then it is a homonym. Chapter on words: with multiple placements, so excluding something like 'I' as a beginning, without an intervening transfer, then it is a homonym. The division of the name with multiple meanings, transferred and real, differs: first regarding the possibility of the homonym, then regarding its occurrence, then regarding it being for opposites. The truth according to the majority of those who affirm the homonym is that the homonym occurs in the speech of the Arabs, indeed in the speech of God, even between opposites, like 'qur'' for menstruation and purity, but there is no generality in it. And here intending all its meanings as real is our position, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him. As for intending all its meanings as one metaphorical meaning, or one... and metaphorical.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the homonym (mushtarak) and discusses its conditions and uses. It begins by stating that if a word has multiple meanings and is placed for each with separate acts of coining, without the word being first used for one meaning and then transferred (i.e., not a transferred term like a proper noun becoming common), then it is a homonym. The text then notes that 'I' as a personal pronoun is excluded because it is a single meaning. The chapter then delves into the division of such names. The author indicates that scholars first consider the possibility of homonyms, then their actual occurrence, and then whether they can be used for opposites. The mainstream view is that homonyms do exist in Arabic and even in the Quran, exemplified by 'qur'' meaning both menstruation and purity (tuhr). However, such homonyms do not imply generalizability across all contexts. The author asserts that when a homonym is used, intending all its meanings simultaneously as real (not metaphorical) is valid in our (Hanafi?) school, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i who reportedly denied it. The text breaks off while discussing intending all meanings as a single metaphorical meaning or as one of them, and the word 'metaphorical'.

Key Terms
المشترك
الوضع
المنقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the example given for a homonym between opposites?
A) 'Asad for lion and brave man.
B) 'Qur' for menstruation and purity.
C) 'Nur' for light and guidance.
D) 'Yad' for hand and power.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak is a word with multiple independent coinings, not a transferred term, and it occurs in Arabic and the Quran, even for opposites like 'qur'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Find the definition of 'qur' in a standard Arabic lexicon. Identify whether it meets the criteria for mushtarak given in this lesson (multiple independent coinings, no transfer).
?
2. Consider the phrase 'إن الله ولي الذين آمنوا' (Allah is the wali of believers). The word 'wali' has multiple meanings (friend, guardian, supporter). Is it a mushtarak? Argue based on the coinage and transfer conditions discussed in the passage.
